To form a tight seal, the valve spring compresses the valve\xe2\x80\x99s O-ring between two planar mating surfaces.\nBoth the valve seat and the sealing surface of the valve head\nare oriented substantially perpendicular to the valve axis. A\npressure differential increases the extent of compression\nbetween the resilient valve seal and both sealing surfaces.\nThe resilient valve seal is an annular O-ring that deforms to\na predetermined extent into sealing engagement against both\nsealing surfaces. When the pressure of a fluid within the vessel exceeds the desired spring biasing force, the valve face lifts\nfrom the valve seat to relieve pressure within the vessel.\nApplications include lab glassware and gloveboxes, or vacuum\ncommercial operations where the content environment must\nbe rigorously controlled.\nGet the complete report on this technology at:\nwww.techbriefs.com/tow/201406a.html\nEmail: nasatech@yet2.com\nPhone: 781-972-0600\n\nA novel technology investigates reservoir beds of oil, gas,\nfreshwater, and thermal water deposits. The system, which\nuses electromagnetic frequency broadband sounding, gathers\ninformation to a depth of up to 7,000 meters. For effective\noperation on deposits, the technology draws up scientifically\nsubstantiated references for the allocation of injection wells.\nThe sounding system discloses and identifies mineral types;\nnonconventional hydrocarbons/shales; and dynamic changes,\nincluding the process of mass movement. Using a helicopter\nor small airplane, deposits in swamps, mountains, and other\nhard-to-reach places can be explored, and a system information model can be constructed. The method is ecologically\nsafe, and searches are achieved in abnormal operation conditions, including crystalline shield, salt beds and pillars.\nGet the complete report on this technology at:\nwww.techbriefs.com/tow/201406b.html\nEmail: nasatech@yet2.com\nPhone: 781-972-0600\n\nTechNeeds \xe2\x80\x94 Requests for Technologies\nTechNeeds are anonymous requests for technologies that you and your organization may be able to fulfill. Responding\nto a TechNeed is the first step to gaining an introduction with a prospective \xe2\x80\x9cbuyer\xe2\x80\x9d for your technology solution.\n\nFabrics with an Inherent Thickness\n\nSterility Verification of a Flowing Liquid\n\nA foam shape must be replaced with a knitted, woven, or\nnonwoven fabric that derives its thickness from the geometric structure of its components. The current application\nuses polyurethane foams that range from millimeters to 2\ncm thick. The foams offer little or no ventilation in a situation where air circulation can be critical. New materials at\nthe fiber level must be used to create a light fabric with a\nbuild-in structural thickness and loft. Possible solution areas\ninclude mathematical models of knitting matrices and nonwoven manufacturing techniques.\nRespond to this TechNeed at:\nwww.techbriefs.com/tn/201406c.html\nEmail: nasatech@yet2.com\nPhone: 781-972-0600\n\nA client seeks a device capable of detecting contamination\nin a liquid flow. The system needs to detect non-liquid particles, bacteria, yeasts, mold, and/or spores in a continuous\nliquid stream. Any proposed sensing methodology must not\nphysically interact with the liquid flowing in the interior of\nthe channel. In operation, the detection system should\nreport any contamination; the nature of the contamination\ncan be determined later. A proposed solution need not identify the specific contaminant. An optical or light-based system\nis desired, but a variety of technologies may be acceptable if\nthey do not require the fluid flow to be sampled directly.\nRespond to this TechNeed at:\nwww.techbriefs.com/tn/201406d.html\nEmail: nasatech@yet2.com\nPhone: 781-972-0600\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n1\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nWe become your strategic partner to \xef\xac\x81nd contract employees who \xef\xac\x81t perfectly.\nYour Aerotek account manager lives and breathes your industry and organization\nand works with you to create a staf\xef\xac\x81ng plan that meets your business needs.\nOur recruiters interview candidates and recommend only the ones who \xef\xac\x81t your\nrequirements to a T. Increase Efficiency!\ndSPACE SCALEXIO\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-706\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nUP\nFRONT\n\nLinda Bell\nEditorial Director\n\nA Step Up for R2\n\n> Last Chance to\n\xe2\x80\x9cCreate the Future\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe lower limbs for the humanoid Robonaut 2 (R2) are\naboard the International Space Station (ISS) awaiting\nattachment by a station crewmember to R2\xe2\x80\x99s torso, which\narrived on the ISS in February 2011 during the last flight\nof the space shuttle Discovery. That event signaled the\nfirst human-like robot to arrive in space to become a permanent resident of the laboratory. Jointly developed by\nNASA and General Motors, R2 showcases how a robotic\nassistant can work alongside humans, whether tasks are\ndone in space or on Earth in a manufacturing facility.\nR2 now consists of a head and a torso with two arms\nand two hands. With the addition of the climbing legs,\nthe robot can augment its chief role: to help astronauts by taking over some of their duties on the space\nstation. But before R2 is up and running with its new\nlimbs, there\xe2\x80\x99s some assembly required.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got a number of upgrades we\xe2\x80\x99re doing,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nRobonaut 2 with his climbing legs.\n(NASA)\nRon Diftler, Robonaut Project Manager at NASA\nJohnson Space Center. \xe2\x80\x9cIn sending up the legs, we also\nhave to change things inside R2\xe2\x80\x99s body.\xe2\x80\x9d That includes new computers, new wiring, mechanical assembly, and interfacing the legs to R2\xe2\x80\x99s main processor. \xe2\x80\x9cWe see about 20 hours of ISS\ncrew time to do the task, following detailed procedures and done over at least a month,\xe2\x80\x9d\nDiftler said.\nRight now, R2\xe2\x80\x99s torso, head, and arms are secured to a stationary base, so crewmembers\ntake tasks to the robot. But getting a \xe2\x80\x9cleg up\xe2\x80\x9d on mobility extends the jobs R2 can perform. \xe2\x80\x9cWe call them the 3 Ds: the dull, dangerous, and dirty,\xe2\x80\x9d Diftler noted. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what\nrobots are for. The astronauts are highly capable individuals that should not have to do all\nthe tasks that require a human-like hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\nWatch Robonaut 2\xe2\x80\x99s climbing legs in motion on Tech Briefs TV at www.techbriefs.com/tv/\nrobonaut-legs. For more information on Robonaut2, go to: http://robonaut.jsc.nasa.gov.\n\nThe 12th annual \xe2\x80\x9cCreate the\nFuture\xe2\x80\x9d Design Contest, sponsored by\nCOMSOL and Mouser Electronics,\nalong with Intel\xc2\xae and Analog Devices,\ncloses for entries on July 1, 2014. If\nyou haven\xe2\x80\x99t submitted your entry, go\nto www.createthefuturecontest.com\ntoday for your chance to receive global recognition and a Grand Prize of\n$20,000 for your new product idea.\nEntries can be submitted in seven different categories. Don\xe2\x80\x99t miss your\nchance to \xe2\x80\x9cCreate the Future.\xe2\x80\x9d Visit\nwww.createthefuturecontest.com to\nsubmit your entry today.\n\nEditor\xe2\x80\x99s Choice\nThe Lunar Organic Waste Reformer (LOWR) converts organic\nwaste from human space exploration outposts into useful\nmethane and oxygen products. It\nreduces risks associated with handling organic wastes while recycling waste into products that otherwise would be imported from\nEarth. In addition, LOWR can be\nused for converting terrestrial\norganic waste into fuel for power\ngeneration. Find out more in the\narticle on page 54.\n\nJet Fuel from Sunlight\nThe European Union-funded Solar chemical reactor demonstration and Optimization for\nLong-term Availability of Renewable JET fuel (SOLAR-JET) project has successfully demonstrated the production chain for renewable kerosene obtained directly from sunlight,\nwater, and carbon dioxide, potentially revolutionizing the future of aviation.\nThe SOLAR-JET project demonstrated an innovative process technology using concentrated sunlight to convert carbon dioxide and water to a so-called synthesis gas (syngas).\nThis is accomplished by means of a redox cycle with metal-oxide based materials at high\ntemperatures. The syngas, a mixture of hydrogen and carbon monoxide, is finally converted into kerosene by using commercial Fischer-Tropsch technology.\nAlthough still at an early stage of development, the processing of syngas to kerosene is\nalready being deployed by companies, including Shell, on a global scale. This combined\napproach has the potential to provide a secure, sustainable, and scalable supply of renewable aviation fuel, and more generally for transport applications. Fischer-Tropsch derived\nkerosene is already approved for commercial aviation.\nVisit www.solar-jet.aero to learn more.\n8\n\n> Next Month in NTB\nThe July issue will feature special\ncoverage on Data Acquisition &\nSensing, as well as new electronics\nproducts on the market.\n\nCONNECT WITH NTB\nfacebook.com/NASATechBriefs\nlinkedin.com/company/\ntech-briefs-media\ntwitter.com/NASATechBriefs\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nAgilent\xe2\x80\x99s Electronic Measurement Group,\nincluding its 9,500 employees and 12,000\nproducts, is becoming Keysight Technologies.\nLearn more at www.keysight.com\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-707\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nWho\xe2\x80\x99s Who at NASA\nDr. Mary Ann Meador, Senior Research Scientist,\nGlenn Research Center, Cleveland, OH\n\nD\n\nr. Mary Ann\nMeador, Senior Research Scientist at NASA Glenn,\nguides projects that\nwill synthesize new\ntypes of aerogels.\nHer research has\nfocused on the design and development of new polymers\nfor a variety of applications, including\nhigh-temperature composites.\n\nNASA Tech Briefs: What are aerogels\nused for?\nDr. Mary Ann Meador: Aerogels are\nlow-density solids with a high degree of\nporosity \xe2\x80\x94 85 to 98%. Different than\nfoams, aerogels have extremely small\npores, on the nanometer scale. This\nmakes them 2 to 5 times better insulators\nthan foams, since gas phase heat conduction is very poor. Since they are com-\n\nprised mostly of air, they also have very\nlow dielectric constants, and hence have\napplication as low-dielectric substrates\nfor devices such as lightweight antennas.\nAerogels also have very large internal surface areas, which means they could also\nbe used as sensor platforms. However,\nthe main application is as insulation for\naerospace, housing, and construction, or\nhigh-performance clothing.\nNTB: What are the weaknesses of previous aerogels?\nDr. Meador: The issue with aerogels is\ntheir fragility. They have a lot of interesting properties. Because of the way\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re made, they have very small pore\nsizes; that leads to really good insulation\nqualities, and also very low thermal conductivities, low dielectric, and high surface areas. Because they also have typically been very fragile, it\xe2\x80\x99s difficult to put\nthem into use as a monolithic material.\n\nNTB: What were your biggest challenges when creating a polyimide aerogel?\nDr. Meador: With the polyimides, our\ngoal was to improve the mechanical properties so that you would retain all the\ninteresting properties of aerogels, but add\nthe mechanical integrity. We actually were\nworking on a project to design a flexible\ninsulation for inflatable aerodynamic\ndecelerators for Entry, Descent and\nLanding (EDL). These are essentially\nlarge structures that inflate in order to\nprovide drag to slow a spacecraft down, or\nto slow down another payload to land on\nthe surface of a planet like Mars or even\nbring things back to Earth. The challenge\nwas to make the aerogel in a flexible form.\nThe insulation has to be able to withstand certain heat of re-entry, so we\nchose polyimide as a very-high-temperature stable polymer. Making the aerogel\nstructure from the polymer gives us the\nability to make thin films of the aerogel.\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s what provides the flexible form of\nthe insulation.\nNTB: What are the possibilities with\nthis new flexibility?\n\nT/TR family of absolute position\nsensors feature repeatability to\n2\xce\xbcm. They are ideal for\nmachine automation\nand control\napplications\nwhere precise repeatability and long life\nare requirements. These sensors offer\na proven solution for applications with\nalignment errors. T/TR position sensors\nare available with a return spring too for\nuse in auto-retraction systems.\n\nNovotechnik\xe2\x80\x99s T/TR family shares\nthese key specs:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRepeatability to \xc2\xb10.002 mm\nStroke lengths from 10 to 150 mm\nLong life of >100 million movements\nLinearity to \xc2\xb10.075%\nPlug or cable connection\n\nFor complete information on T/TR sensors visit www.novotechnik.com/ttr\nNovotechnik U.S., Inc.\n155 Northboro Road \xe2\x80\xa2 Southborough, MA 01772\nTelephone: 508-485-2244 Fax: 508-485-2430\n\nWant to learn more?\nRegister for a live webcast on NASA\nGlenn\xe2\x80\x99s aerogel technology featuring Dr. Meador on June 19. Go to\nwww.techbriefs.com/webinar214\nto register for the free presentation.\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-708\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\n10\n\nDr. Meador: If you can make a thin\nfilm that can now be wrapped around\nstructures, it\xe2\x80\x99s a much more conventional way to deliver insulation. Thin films\ncould be used to wrap around a cryotank, to wrap around pipes that are\nhandling cryogenic liquids, or they can\nbe wrapped around things that you want\nto keep heat in. We think the commercial potential for other types of polymer\naerogels would be higher since they may\nbe more cost-competitive with conventional insulation. We are working on\npolyimide aerogels as an alternative,\nlower-cost option.\nTo read a full transcript of the interview or\nlisten to a downloadable podcast, visit\nwww.techbriefs.com/podcast.\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\nIn\xef\xac\x81nite Designs, One Platform\nwith the only complete\nsystem design environment\n\nNI LabVIEW is the only comprehensive development environment with the\n\nLabVIEW system\ndesign software offers\nunrivaled hardware\nintegration and helps\nyou program the way\nyou think\xe2\x80\x93graphically.\n\nunprecedented hardware integration and wide-ranging compatibility you need to meet\nany measurement and control application challenge. The agency has a portfolio of 3000 patents and pending applications available now\nfor license by businesses and individuals, including these recently patented inventions:\n\nNon-Collinear Valve Actuator\nU.S. Patent No. 8,322,685\n\nJames A. Richard, Marshall Space\nFlight Center, AL\nTo regulate the flow of a quarter-turn\nvalve, the weight of a flight system\xe2\x80\x99s valve\nactuator must be reduced. Previous actuators have placed a pressure-actuated\npiston and return spring system exclusively in line with one another. The\nweight of return spring systems in the\nvalve actuators, therefore, has depended\nupon the piston\xe2\x80\x99s displacement.\nA non-collinear valve actuator includes\na primary actuating system and a return\nspring system. Both systems act on different points of a rotably affixed transmission link, which is one component of a\nlinkage technology responsible for opening and closing a quarter-turn valve.\nHaving the primary actuating system\napply force at a greater radial distance\nthan the return spring system increases\nstroke length while minimizing displacement. By allowing the primary actuating\nsystem to undergo longer strokes, the\nweight of the primary actuating system\nmay be optimized. Furthermore, the\nnon-collinear positioning and diminished displacement reduces the weight\nof the return spring system and ultimately the weight of the valve actuator.\n\nUltraminiature Broadband\nLight Source with Spiral\nShaped Filament\nU.S. Patent No. 8,264,134\n\nJoseph S. Collura, Henry Helvajian,\nMichael Pocha, Glenn A. Meyer,\nCharles F. McConaghy, Barry L. Olsen,\nand William W. Hansen, Glenn\nResearch Center, Cleveland, OH\nWhite light sources are used to calibrate spectrometers in the visible range.\nIt is desirable, therefore, to have an output source that spans past the visible\nlight spectrum: red, orange, yellow,\ngreen, blue, indigo, and violet.\n\nAn ultraminiature light source features a generally planar, double-spiralshaped tungsten filament suspended\nwithin a ceramic base. A lid, which is partially transparent, is placed over the\nceramic base. The lid may also be coated\nwith dielectric material to selectively\ntransfer specific radiation bands that are\nselective to the application. The ceramic\nenclosure includes a reflective bottom, a\nledge, and a raised perimeter having a\nmetallic surface. The ledge features\nmetallic surfaces embedded therein for\nelectrical communication with the double-spiral shaped tungsten filament.\n\nVariably Transmittive,\nElectronically Controlled\nEyewear\nU.S. Patent No. 8,411,214\n\nJohn J. Chapman, Louis J. Glaab,\nTimothy D. Schott, Charles T. Howell,\nand Vincent J. Fleck, Langley\nResearch Center, Hampton, VA\nSpecific pilot training and evaluation\nprocedures for instrument flight rating\nqualification currently involve the use of\nan opaque hood draped across an aircraft\xe2\x80\x99s windscreen. The hood restricts or\nobscures the student pilot\xe2\x80\x99s vision during instrument-only flight sessions.\nThere is a need for a training technique\nthat restricts the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s field of view\nwhile providing a more realistic and\nsafer training experience.\nA flight training and evaluation system\nincludes electronically activated vision\nrestriction glasses that detect the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s\nhead position and automatically darken\nand limit the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s ability to see\nthrough the front and side windscreens.\nThus, the pilot-in-training sees only within the aircraft cockpit, forcing him or\nher to fly with instruments in the most\nrestricted operational mode.\n\nFor more information on the inventions described here, contact the appropriate NASA\nField Center\xe2\x80\x99s Innovative Partnerships (IP) Office. It\xe2\x80\x99s 3D printing,\nand it is shaping the future of manufacturing. NASA Tech Briefs spoke recently with executives at\nfour of the leading 3D printer vendors about what 3D printing is today, what it will be tomorrow,\nand if it really will change the world.\nOur roundtable panel members are Jeff Moe, Founder and CEO of Aleph Objects; Bre Pettis, CEO\nof MakerBot; Greg Mark, CEO of MarkForged; Conor MacCormack, Co-founder and CEO of Mcor\nTechnologies; and Jon Cobb, Executive Vice President of Corporate Affairs for Stratasys.\n\nNASA Tech Briefs: 3D printing is being\nreferred to as the next industrial revolution. Is\nit really on a par with where the personal computer was in the 1980s \xe2\x80\x93 on the cusp of being\na ubiquitous technology that pervades both\nour work and home lives, or is that democratization of the technology still years away?\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c\n\nGreg Mark: Nobody knows. Technology has a way of sneaking up on you,\nbut it\xe2\x80\x99s impossible to predict exactly when\nthat will occur, or how people will use it.\nBre Pettis: We do see the 3D printing\nindustry paralleling the personal computer industry. Thirty years ago, consumers\nused to ask the question, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy do I need a\ncomputer in my home? I have a calculator.\xe2\x80\x9d Today, everyone has a computer more\npowerful than we ever dreamed of right in\nhis or her pocket \xe2\x80\x93 their smartphone. We see 3D printing going in\nthe same direction.\nJeff Moe: 3D printing technology is a few years away from\nbecoming ubiquitous. Today, we do have customers around the\nworld using our machines, but right now, the 3D printing market reminds me of Linux in the 1990s. For us, ease of use cannot and will not come at the expense of making good hardware.\nJon Cobb: 3D printing could be the next industrial revolution because it has a broader impact. Individuals have the capability to design and print personal products. Embracing this\ninnovative technology will fuel not only a change in the design\nand manufacturing process, but also distribution.\nConor MacCormack: I relish our industry\xe2\x80\x99s challenge of creating a 3D printer for everyone, an ecosystem to support it, and\nthe true democratization of innovation. A new group of users,\neveryday consumers, are less technically skilled, less driven to\nmaster every aspect of 3D printing, but just as interested in the\ntechnology\xe2\x80\x99s ability to make things they need.\nNTB: There is a new job title in design and manufacturing called\n\xe2\x80\x9cmaker.\xe2\x80\x9d How does that incorporate what the designer and engineer currently do, and what does the \xe2\x80\x9cmaker\xe2\x80\x9d do that is a separate function?\nCobb: I believe today\xe2\x80\x99s makers are what we called \xe2\x80\x9ccraftsmen\xe2\x80\x9d in the past. Makers look at a problem and design a\n14\n\nWe focus on customer\nfeedback \xe2\x80\x93 people looking to\nprint parts with the strength\nof metal, at their desk. That\nwill enable thousands of\nengineers to fabricate parts\nwhen they need them, without waiting in a long queue\nfor machine time.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nGreg Mark\nMarkForged\n\nsolution. The 3D printer, along with software that is becoming much easier to use, allows the maker to solve his or her\nown problem, on their own terms, and within their own\ntimeframe.\nMark: I suspect it means different things to different people.\nIn the consumer space, it may be a synonym for \xe2\x80\x9chobbyist.\xe2\x80\x9d In\nthe industrial space, it may be a synonym for fabricator.\nMacCormack: Makers are technically savvy early adopters\nwho have gone online, purchased hobbyist 3D printers, and\ntinkered with the technology. Many makers are designers and\nengineers, but not all. These are the people who constantly\npush the envelope of what the technology can do for them\nboth professionally and personally.\nMoe: As the maker movement gets more sophisticated, it follows that companies are creating positions for these types of\ntalented individuals with diverse skill sets. Generally, we understand makers to be inquisitive self-starters who aren\xe2\x80\x99t afraid to\nget their hands dirty. Ultimately, a maker\xe2\x80\x99s job function will\ndepend on what companies need.\nPettis: Today, with the help of 3D printing, creative individuals are now being heralded as the new \xe2\x80\x9cmakers\xe2\x80\x9d of our\ngeneration. Their talents and skills to move ideas from concept to reality through the process of making are being celebrated. 3D printing brings innovation and iteration to the\nforefront.\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMULTIBODY DYNAMICS: Model of a three-cylinder\n\nVERIFY AND OPTIMIZE YOUR DESIGNS WITH\n\nCOMSOL MULTIPHYSICS\n\n\xc2\xae\n\nMultiphysics tools let you build simulations that accurately\nreplicate the important characteristics of your designs. On the contrary, we\xe2\x80\x99re working on new ways to print 3D objects in our\nR&D labs that, if successful, will really cause a revolution, not\njust an evolution.\nPettis: MakerBot is currently committed to FDM 3D printing. Our focus is about making 3D printing affordable and\naccessible on your desktop, and FDM is the safest, most versatile form of 3D printing.\nCobb: I believe all other companies are working to deliver more materials that are well defined in their strength,\nflexibility, and other properties. I think PolyJet technology\ngives us good insight into the future where 3D printers can\nprint multiple materials with colors, clear or opaque, and\nsmooth or textured surfaces. In short, it\xe2\x80\x99s manufacturing in\na box.\n\n3D printing technology\nis a few years away from\nbecoming ubiquitous. Right\nnow, the 3D printing market\nreminds me of Linux in the\n1990s. For us, ease of use cannot and will not come at the\nexpense of making good\nhardware.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNTB: Industry has seen the introduction of 3D printers that use a\nvariety of materials \xe2\x80\x93 or multiple materials in one machine. How do\nmanufacturers decide, in many cases, between function and aesthetics\nwhen selecting the right machine and material?\nMoe: Manufacturers should not be forced to choose a\nmachine because it comes with a certain proprietary printing\nmaterial, or is designed to only use one type of printing material. Material developers around the world are consistently innovating and we enjoy bringing their new products to our community. When experimental materials face constraints, developers\nwill introduce new tools and accessories to overcome them.\nMacCormack: Users will dictate applications, and applications will dictate material requirements. For example, we\nselected ordinary paper as the primary build medium for our\n3D printers because people couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get access to the technology. They demanded much lower cost and ubiquitous consumables,. However, if you want to make an injector nozzle for an\nengine, the real-world loading will dictate the material and\nhence the technology.\nMark: We focused on customer feedback \xe2\x80\x94 people looking\nto print parts with the strength of metal, at their desk. That will\nenable thousands of engineers to fabricate parts when they\nneed them, without waiting in a long queue for machine time.\nPettis: We focus on desktop 3D printing and because of that,\nwe also focus on safe and easy-to-use\nmaterial that can be used in the office,\nhome, or classroom. We empower individuals to be creative right at their desk.\nCobb: Manufacturers are looking to\nsolve a specific problem. Frequently,\n3D printing is seen as a potential solution. 3D printing is similar to many\nmanufacturing processes that are\nemployed today, and these processes\ncan and often rely on secondary\nprocesses. The part material takes\nprecedent.\n\nNTB: We\xe2\x80\x99re seeing the evolution of the technology in 3D printing\nmachines. What about the evolution on the software side? The printer, designed and built by Made in Space\n(Moffett Field, CA), will be able to print parts from files sent to the crew on the ISS. We recently spoke with\nMichael Snyder, Director of R&D for Made in Space, and Niki Werkheiser, 3D Printing in Zero-G Project Manager\nin the Technology Development and Transfer Office at NASA\xe2\x80\x99s Marshall Space Flight Center (Alabama).\nNASA Tech Briefs: How does the printer differ from a commercial 3D printer?\nMichael Snyder: We\nstarted off doing simulated flights in the zero-g parabolic aircraft with the\nambition to modify commercial printers to work in\nmicrogravity. Through a\ncouple days of testing, we\nrealized that the things\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d end up modifying to\nmake the systems work\nMichael Snyder, Director of R&D\nwere pretty substantial. It\nfor Made in Space. (Emmett Given/\nNASA MSFC)\nended up being a lot better for us to build it from\nthe ground up because of the differences from microgravity to Earth gravity. The process itself is basically the same.\nThere was no \xe2\x80\x9ceureka\xe2\x80\x9d moment in terms of the process\nbeing totally different. The mechanics are slightly different, and those slight differences cause substantial defects in\nthe prints.\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to email parts to space as soon as the printer is installed in the glovebox. We\xe2\x80\x99ll be in our offices in\nSilicon Valley, be able to hit \xe2\x80\x9csend,\xe2\x80\x9d and the part file will be\non the printer in a matter of seconds.\nNTB: How are NASA and Made in Space working together on\nthis project?\nNiki Werkheiser: Made\nin Space, although they\nare making the first 3D\nprinter in space, is interested in the longer term\nand creating an industry.\nEnabling space exploration is the ultimate goal.\nTo enable exploration,\nthe key first step would be\nto manufacture and have\nindependence from Earthbased launches \xe2\x80\x93 to manufacture the part you need\non demand. All of the\nflight certification and mission testing for the printer\nis occurring at NASA, and\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re packing it so it\xe2\x80\x99s safe\nfor launch.\n\nThe goal is to take a plastic part made out of the feedstock materials and recycle it back into filament. That will\nbe a very exciting day in space when you can do that,\nbecause then you\xe2\x80\x99re actually showing the first concept of\ntrue independent sustainability. Imagine a day when we\ncan take old food packaging and recycle it back into feedstock material. It\xe2\x80\x99s not that far off.\nNTB: Are there other applications for this printer?\nSnyder: We see this technology being very useful for a\nwide variety of applications \xe2\x80\x93 not just based on space. We\xe2\x80\x99re\nin conversations with a lot of people in pretty much every\nindustry and government arm you can think of. It\xe2\x80\x99s really\nexciting and this is relatively brand new \xe2\x80\x94 thinking of this\ntechnology as more than just a toy. We can really revolutionize how things are made and how quickly they can be made.\nImagine in war, your Humvee breaks down but you have a\nprinter. You can fix your Humvee and be back on the road\nin a matter of hours instead of waiting for people to come\nhelp you.\nWerkheiser: The Navy is very interested in its use in\nsubmarines. Obviously, it\xe2\x80\x99s very remote, like the space station, and we\xe2\x80\x99ve been working together with Made in\nSpace with these entities. Ultimately, to develop the technology, our endpoint applications may be different, but\nthere is overlap in the core set of capabilities we both\nneed to see developed.\nNTB: This project will reduce the distance goods will need to go,\nand reduce launch costs?\n\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Niki Werkheiser holds a 3D\nprinted Cubesat structure, one of\nthe many potential applications\nthat an in-space manufacturing\ncapability will provide. (Emmett\nGiven/NASA MSFC)\n\n22\n\nSnyder: Absolutely. That\xe2\x80\x99s what we set out to do is disrupt\nthe supply chain and make it more convenient, eventually\neliminating the need for launch vehicles. When you\xe2\x80\x99re out\nexploring places like Mars, you won\xe2\x80\x99t have the capability\nyou have on the station. Imagine going to Mars and having\nfeedstock that you can transform into backup environmental control systems or refrigerators. You can do that with\nthe same material and not have to store spares, which\nreduces mass.\nRight now, this technology is capable of doing great\nthings, and it needs to be viewed the same way we view a drill\npress or a CNC machine. It\xe2\x80\x99s actually better because of the\nextended capability it gives you. It will be a matter of time\nbefore people finally open their eyes fully and realize how\nmuch this changes everything.\nFor more information, visit www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/\nstation/research/experiments/1115.html or www.madeinspace.com.\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nNew C-more Touch Panels\n\xc2\xae\n\n$\n\nHigher Performance, Lower Price\n\n6\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n00\n\n499.\n\nu.s.\n\nEA9-T6CL-R\nSerial only\n\n$\n\n10\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n\n1290.00\n\nu.s.\n\nEA9-T10CL\nSerial and Ethernet\n\n6\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n$\n00\n\n699.\n\nu.s.\nEA9-T6CL\nSerial and Ethernet\n\n12\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n\n$\n\n8\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n$\n00\n\n999.\n\n1790.00\n\nu.s.\n\nEA9-T12CL\nSerial and Ethernet\n\nu.s.\n\nEA9-T8CL\nSerial and Ethernet\n\n$\n\n15\xe2\x80\x9d Color TFT\n\n1999.00\n\nu.s.\n\nEA9-T15CL\nSerial and Ethernet\n\nThe popular C-more HMIs have\nALWAYS been a great value, but now\nthey are better than ever. The spacecraft is being\npowered by a combination of solar arrays and two advanced,\nspace-qualified, 28-Volt, 55-Ah Yardney lithium-ion batteries.\nThe MAVEN spacecraft was launched on an Atlas V-401\nrocket, which also includes 12 Yardney Silver Zinc batteries\n(booster main 2, FTS 4, and Pyro 6). The lithium-ion batteries\nhave additionally powered the Mars Rover, Phoenix Mars\nLander, and other satellites.\nThe MAVEN\xe2\x80\x99s solar arrays produce 1,150 Watts of energy to\npower the spacecraft and its payload. The spacecraft has several scientific instruments to study solar winds, magnetic field,\nand other characteristics of the Martian upper atmosphere.\nThe lithium-ion battery stores extra energy at times when\ndemand is low, provides extra power when demand is high,\nand is used for load leveling and transient suppression in the\npower system.\nFor Free Info Visit http://info.hotims.com/49746-116\n\nE ZVI Technology Cleans Up\nContaminants at Kennedy\nSpace Center\nJacobs Engineering Group\nPasadena, CA\nwww.jacobs.com\nCORE Engineering and Construction\nWinter Park, FL\nwww.core-encon.com\nA groundwater technology developed at Kennedy Space\nCenter was used to treat subsurface contaminants near one of\nthe center\xe2\x80\x99s buildings: the Reutilization, Recycling and\nMarketing Facility (RRMF).\nThe RRMF was constructed in the late 1960s for the storage\nand recycling of a variety of equipment and chemicals. The target was the chlorinated solvent tetrachloroethene, also known as PERC, which had been historically released into the environment.\nRemediation Project Manager Anne Chrest of Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s\nCenter Operations Directorate led the cleanup efforts. However, most pilots/autonomous systems are unable to adapt to the altered\nconfiguration and learn to control the\ndamaged aircraft in the very short time\navailable for safe operation. Fortunately,\nthe flight computer may have the necessary information as well as bandwidth\navailable to learn the new dynamics and\ndetermine mechanisms to control the\nvehicle quickly. The flight computer needs\nan intelligent controller that flies the vehicle with the baseline controller during\nnormal conditions, and adapts the design\nwhen the vehicle suffers damage. Using\ninformation about the vehicle from all the\navailable sensors, the system determines\nwhether the vehicle is damaged. Direct\nadaptive control (DAC) looks directly at\nthe errors, and updates the control law\n\naccordingly. This technology looks not just\nat the tracking error, but rather its characteristics over time to determine whether\nthe controller needs to be adapted or left\nalone. This is typically fast and meets the\ntiming considerations for aircraft/spacecraft system implementation.\nThis technology presents a novel, stable, discrete-time adaptive law for flight\ncontrol in a DAC framework. Where\nerrors are not present, the original control design has been tuned for optimal\nperformance. Adaptive control works\ntowards achieving nominal performance\nwhenever the design has modeling\nuncertainties/errors or when the vehicle\nsuffers substantial flight configuration\nchange. The baseline controller uses\ndynamic inversion with proportionalintegral augmentation. On-line adaptation of this control law is achieved by\nproviding a parameterized augmentation signal to a dynamic inversion block.\nThe parameters of this augmentation\n\nsignal are updated to achieve the nominal desired error dynamics.\nIf the system senses that at least one\naircraft component is experiencing an\nexcursion and the return of this component value toward its reference value is\nnot proceeding according to the expected controller characteristics, then the\nneural network (NN) modeling of aircraft operation may be changed.\nThis work was done by John T. Kaneshige,\nKalmanje S. Krishnakumar, and Nilesh V.\nKulkarni of Ames Research Center; and John\nBurken of Dryden Flight Research Center. For\nmore information, download the Technical\nSupport Package (free white paper) at\nwww.techbriefs.com/tsp under the Physical\nSciences category.\nNASA invites companies to inquire about\npartnering opportunities. Contact the Ames\nTechnology Partnerships Office at 1-855-6272249 or ARC-TechTransfer@mail.nasa.gov.\nRefer to ARC-16235-1.\n\nDetecting an Extreme Minority Class in Hyperspectral Data\nUsing Machine Learning\nAutomated classifiers can detect surface sulfur in orbital remote sensing observations.\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, California\nOrbital remote sensing provides a powerful way to efficiently survey targets for\nfeatures of interest in inaccessible regions\nof the Earth as well as on other planets.\nOne such feature of astrobiological relevance is the presence of surface sulfur\ndeposits, which may be present on icy\nmoons such as Europa. All hyperspectral\ninstruments face the difficult task of spectral feature selection (finding the spectral\nbands that matter), especially those that\noperate in previously unstudied arenas\nencountered in planetary missions. This\nsoftware demonstrates how manually annotated labels can enable automated feature\ndiscovery that boosts science return.\n\nThis work evaluates the ability of automated classifiers to detect sulfur in\nremote sensing observations by the\nHyperion spectrometer on the EO-1\n(Earth Observing-1) spacecraft. A datadriven machine learning solution was\nrequired because it is not possible to reliably detect sulfur in hyperspectral data by\nsimply matching observations to sulfur lab\nspectra, as is common for in-situ mineral\nimaging. Several methods (manual and\nautomated) were evaluated to select the\nmost relevant attributes (spectral bands)\nfor successful sulfur detection.\nData was taken from an Earthly analog\nfor Europa: a northern island where\n\n26\n\nbioactivity has produced surface sulfur\ndeposits on ice. This software uses\nmachine learning algorithms to automatically discover the best spectral features\nthat discriminate interesting spectra from\nuninteresting surrounding spectra such\nas observations of ice, rock, and exposed\nsulfur deposits. Unique aspects of this\nstudy include ground validation using terrestrial sulfuric ice springs, modern\nmachine learning feature detection using\na Support Vector Machine (SVM), and\nreal-time execution onboard the EO-1\nspacecraft. A primary technical innovation is the ability to handle a severe imbalance between extremely rare positive\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMeasure Any Sensor.\n10X Accuracy, 10X Throughput\nThan Any Other.\nV\n\nI\n\nThe DT9829 provides 8 multi-sensor input channels.\nUse any sensor on any channel. Kennedy Space Center,\nFlorida\n\nUse Java\xe2\x84\xa2 and Android\xe2\x84\xa2\nfor the Coolest DAQ Apps\n\nThe Spaceport Weather Data Archive\nprovides a fully searchable database of\nweather data gathered at Kennedy Space\nCenter and the Cape Canaveral Air Force\nStation. Weather data includes wind, temperature, and humidity data from a surface meteorological tower network; upper\nair soundings from both weather balloons\nand radar wind profilers; and data from\nthe extensive rain gauge network. Not\nonly can the user easily retrieve data and\ndownload it, but the user can also view\ngraphically the weather data on a map\noverlay. For example, the user can enter\nsearch criteria to view all lightning strikes\nending at a particular date/time, and\ngraphically see the lightning strikes colorcoded based on elapsed time for the prior\nseven hours. A unique feature of the software is the capability to invoke an automated playback for cloud-to-ground lightning events on a geographic overlay for a\nselected date and time interval.\nThis innovation separates the publicfacing Web application from the\nKSC/CCAFS internal Web application\nsoftware, and has eliminated ftp downloads. The look and feel has been\ndesigned to be similar to current state-ofthe-art weather research Web sites, and\nprovides common, standardized interfaces for the various weather data types.\nThe archive has been designed for the\nhandling of new data for automated quality control (empty files, duplicate data,\nreasonableness verification, and validity\nchecks).\nThe data are updated every 60 minutes for certain instruments, and as\nfrequently as every 15 minutes for oth-\n\nWireless DAQ/Tablet Bundle\n\xe2\x80\xa2 BTH-1208LS multifunction\nBluetooth\xe2\x84\xa2 DAQ device\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Google\xe2\x84\xa2 Nexus\xe2\x84\xa2 7 Tablet\nincluded\n\xe2\x80\xa2 UL for Android Library\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Example programs\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Ready-to-run Demo Apps\navailable at Google Play\xe2\x84\xa2\n\nCollect data\nwirelessly with the\nBTH-1208LS DAQ\ndevice\n\nBest Deal of the Year \xe2\x80\x93 Only $429\nmccdaq.com/BTHBundle\nContact us\nThe Value Leader in Data Acquisition\n\n1.800.234.4232\n\nTablet photo used with permission \xc2\xa9 Google Inc. GSC-16402-1\n\nSystems and Services for Near-Real-Time Web Access to NPP Data\nSoftware for processing and interpreting S-NPP observations and related data has become\npublicly available and more readily usable.\nMarshall Space Flight Center, Alabama\nThe recently launched Suomi National Polar-orbiting Partnership (SNPP) satellite, operated by NASA and\nthe National Oceanic and Atmospheric\nAdministration(NOAA), is providing\nmultispectral global observations over\nthe next several years to support a broad\narray of research and applications. SNPP data products consist of a complex\nset of data and metadata files in highly\nspecialized formats, and the U.S. government\xe2\x80\x99s operational ground segment\ndelivers these to users with delays of several hours to a few days.\nA growing set of antennas around the\nglobe is capable of receiving S-NPP\xe2\x80\x99s\ncontinuous, unencrypted data broad-\n\nple sources over the Internet, processes\nthem as quickly as possible using a scalable cloud computing environment,\nand delivers data products and visualizations on demand via standard Web\nservices that can interoperate with a\nvariety of end-user display and analysis\ntools.\nThe first component of the system (a\nRetrieval server) monitors data repositories at several remote S-NPP and\nAqua MODIS receiving sites and fetches new satellite data files as soon as they\nbecome available. The second component (one or more Processing servers)\nruns science-validated algorithms on\nthe newly fetched data, and prepares\ndata products and visualizations for\nWeb presentation to end users. The\nthird component (a Map/Data server)\ndraws on these data and visual products, using industry-standard protocols\nto serve a variety of software clients.\nEach component is distributed as an\nintegrated machine image, ready to be\ninstantiated on virtual machines in a\ncloud computing environment. As virtual machines, the three components\ncan be reconfigured easily to monitor\ndifferent data sources, and to perform\ndifferent processing workflows on the\nretrieved data.\nOne advantage is the use of cloud\ncomputing for low-cost entry into fullscale scientific computing and radical\nscalability to meet wide variations in processing demand at moderate costs.\nAnother is the use of virtual machines\ncapable of running many different workflows, easily reconfigured to fetch,\nprocess, and deliver a variety of products\nfrom distributed sources.\nThis work was done by John Evans,\nEduardo Valente, Wei Hao, and Samir\nChettri of Global Science & Technology, Inc.\nfor Marshall Space Flight Center. For more\ninformation, contact Sammy Nabors, MSFC\nCommercialization Assistance Lead at\nsammy.a.nabors@nasa.gov. Refer to MFS33076-1.\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-725\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\n32\n\ncast, and sharing the raw data via the\nInternet. The TDC can be used to eliminate\ncostly ground support equipment by\nplacing the telemetry decoding functions in an inexpensive, commercially\navailable field programmable gate array\n(FPGA) integrated circuit instead of\nspecial-purpose printed circuit boards.\nThe TDC can also be used in the design\nof telemetry systems by enabling end-toend simulation of these systems\xe2\x80\x99 upfront simulation before any hardware is\nbuilt. The TDC was developed for the\nGlobal Precipitation Measurement\n(GPM) project and because of its success on that project, it will be used to\nverify telemetry on the Magnetospheric\nMultiscale (MMS) project.\nThe TDC has a wide range of flexibility as shown in the figure. A num-\n\nFEATURES\n\nber of options can be enabled or\nbypassed including: a \xc2\xbd rate Viterbi\ndecoder, a NRZM Converter, a bit\ninterleaver, a Reed Solomon decoder,\npseudo derandomization, and a CRC\nchecker. The Viterbi decoder can be\neither hard or soft decision and can\nresync itself based upon a user-configurable bit error rate (BER). The TDC\nalso does frame synchronization and\ncan detect standard pseudorandom\npatterns PN7, PN15, and PN23. It can\naccommodate a dual source system by\nprocessing two sets of frame data\nstreams.\nThis work was done by Thomas Winkert\nand Omar Haddad of Goddard Space Flight\nCenter, and Iraj Sardari of MEI Technologies.\nFor more information, download the\nTechnical Support Package (free white\npaper) at www.techbriefs.com/tsp under the\nElectronics/Computers category. The\nswitches are intended to handle kiloamplevel currents and may dissipate peak\npowers measured in megawatts. Recent\nexperiments measured the response of a\nswitch module composed of eight\nSGTOs to single-short, high-current pulses. Simulations of those experiments\nwere performed to calculate the temperature changes that occur inside the\n\ndevices, where measurement is not possible. Worst-case operating conditions in\nwhich the switches handle several pulses\nwithin the space of 4 or 5 seconds (s) also\nwere simulated. Modeling and simulation were performed with SolidWorks 3D\nmodeling software and SolidWorks\nSimulation computational fluid dynamics software from Dassault Syst\xc3\xa8mes.\nThe original switch model includes a\nmodule composed of eight SGTO die,\nlugs and spacers, and base, as well as an\n\na\n\nb\n\nThe simplified Super Gate Turn-Off Thyristor Switch models in (a) full model, and (b) quarter model.\nThe model was simplified, and simulation run times reduces, by taking advantage of the symmetry of\nthe model and using one-quarter of the simplified model for simulation.\n\naluminum cold plate to conduct heat\nfrom the module. In order to reduce the\ncomputation time spent in thermal simulations, the original model was simplified,\nreducing the curves and fillets to a geometry suitable to a rectangular mesh, while\nattempting to preserve the dimensions\nand volume of the parts (and materials)\nfor time-dependent thermal calculations.\nThe model was further simplified, and\nsimulation run times reduced, by taking\nadvantage of the symmetry of the model\nand using one-quarter of the simplified\nmodel for simulation. Spacers were eliminated from the model; they have no significant influence on the thermal\nresponse of the switch in the timespans\nstudied (see figure).\nThe initial temperature of the model\nand of the air surrounding it was defined to\nbe 70 \xc2\xb0C. The bottom surface of the cold\nplate on the switch module was also defined\nto be 70 \xc2\xb0C; this served as a sink for heat\ngenerated in the SGTOs. The top surface\n\nAIRPLANES\nSHOULDN\xe2\x80\x99T LEAVE\nFOOTPRINTS\n\nKARL KITTS\nHigh performance\nrelays, contactors &\npower distribution\nsolutions\n\nJet fuel is a major expense for airlines, so saving even a fraction per \xef\xac\x82ight can add up to millions of dollars in\nsavings. Other logos, product and/or Company names might be trademarks of their respective owners.\n\nEVERY CONNECTION COUNTS\n\n36\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-729\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nof the circuit board was subject to natural\nconvection, which was included in the simulation by defining those surfaces to have a\nconvection coefficient of 10 W/m2-K.\nThe time-dependent thermal inputs for\nthe simulations were derived from measurements of voltage drops and current in\nthe SGTOs made during experimental\ncharacterizations performed with the solidstate switch modules. Curves of power versus time were culled from much larger files\nof data recorded at 0.1-microsecond (\xce\xbcs)\nintervals. It was assumed that the switch\nmodule distributes the power equally\namong the SGTOs. In the simulation,\npower was applied uniformly throughout\nthe active part of the die, rather than applying it solely to a junction within the die.\nWhile the active part of the die reached\na temperature in excess of 130 \xc2\xb0C, that part\nof the die is covered with a lid, and the\nobservable part of the module has reached\na maximum temperature of about 100 \xc2\xb0C.\nLittle of the heat in the first pulse has been\nconducted outside the active die in 35 \xce\xbcs.\nThe \xe2\x80\x9coff\xe2\x80\x9d time is 1.66 s, at the end of which\nthe die has almost returned to its initial\ntemperature of 70 \xc2\xb0C.\nNext, how the temperature of the\nSGTOs rose and fell during a series of pulses was calculated. Two cases were used. The\nfirst was five 35- s pulses in a 4-s period, and\nthe second was four 35- s pulses in a 5-s\nperiod to see whether or not the temperature of the devices stays within a safe operational range. SGTOs return almost to the\noriginal baseline temperature between\npulses, and the average temperature rises\nwith each pulse to just over 130 \xc2\xb0C. Silicon\ndevices may not operate as efficiently at\nsuch temperatures as they do at room temperature, but are not likely to be damaged\nby such short transits to high temperatures.\nThere still is little sign of heat spreading\nthrough the module after 5 s, and visible\ntemperatures are within one degree of\nthose at the peak of the first pulse.\nThe second set of calculations used a\n54-\xce\xbcs-wide pulse as a thermal input to the\nSGTO switch model. Peak power is 1.5\nMW per SGTO, assuming equal distribution of power among the SGTOs in the\nmodule. As with the 35-ms pulse case, the\nthermal effects of a single pulse, a series\nof five pulses in 4 s, and four pulses in 5\ns were calculated. The longer pulse raises\nthe device temperature over 140 \xc2\xb0C,\nabout 10 \xc2\xb0C higher than the 35-ms pulse.\nThe effects of 4- and 5-pulse trains with\nthe 54-ms pulse were calculated. As each\npulse was applied, the average temperature in the active part of the SGTOs rose\nto more than 140 \xc2\xb0C, and returned\nalmost to the baseline temperature\n\nbetween pulses. The SGTO temperature\nat the end of the last pulse in both cases\nwas approximately 144 \xc2\xb0C.\nThe model shows a quick rise in the temperature of the SGTOs during the pulse,\nbut peak temperatures are within safe\noperating limits for Si devices. The heat in\nthe devices dissipates quickly into the surrounding package and into the cold plate\nto which it is mounted. Heat conduction\noccurs rapidly enough that the devices\xe2\x80\x99\ntemperature returns very close to their initial temperature within about 1 s after the\nend of the pulse. Calculations predict that\n\nthe peak temperatures in the SGTOs will\nincrease little over time when a series of\nshort pulses over 4 or 5 s is applied. This\nindicates that the switches should be able\nto withstand repeated cycling at high\npower levels without sustaining heat-related damage or suffering serious degradation of performance.\nThis work was done by Gregory K. Ovrebo\nof the Army Research Laboratory. For more\ninformation, download the Technical\nSupport Package (free white paper) at\nwww.techbriefs.com/tsp under the Electronics/Computers category. This is accomplished by combining solar panel aspect\nratio and edge features with simple\nspacecraft packaging optimization and\nintegrated thermal-analysis techniques\nthat also allow specifying a more benign\ntemperature corridor.\nThere has been a perceived general\naerobraking risk in early-stage mission\ndesigns. The aero-heating of the solar\npanels during aerobraking in the warmer\nVenus thermal environment is considered riskier than Mars aerobraking.\nMost missions were not optimized for\naerobraking, particularly the solar-panel\nand spacecraft-shape aspect ratio and\nthermal features. Mars aerobraking had\nmore uncertainty, but lower solar heating rates and more actual mission data.\nVenus has more solar heating, higher\ninitial temperature conditions, and\nfewer real-life examples, but less atmos-\n\npheric change. Lingering aerobraking\nrisks applied to such missions in the\nearly stage required more aggressive\nearly-phase reduction of risks. Older\ndesigns did not pursue aggressive solar\npanel thermal load reduction features,\nor optimization of temperature, and\ninstead focused on heat rate corridors.\nThis approach combines multiple new\nfeatures into one combined solution.\nThe first feature is lower heating from\npast designs by applying the lowest\naspect ratio solar-panel layout, resulting\nin lowest heating for the solar-panel area\nrequired for the mission. A new \xe2\x80\x9cpicture\nframe\xe2\x80\x9d edge is added to solar panels.\nThe outside 3 to 6 in. (\xe2\x89\x887.6 to 15.2 cm)\nof the panels are directed 45 to 30\xc2\xb0 into\nthe flow. This reduces heating by an\nadditional 15%. The projected drag surface is increased, which reduces the ballistic coefficient that can be used to\neither decrease aerobraking duration or\nincrease thermal margin. These edges,\nwhich receive higher heat flux, are pro-\n\ntected with high-temperature multi-layer\ninsulation.\nThe new high-temperature solar panel\nfeatures high-temperature graphite epoxy\nfacesheet and solar array design. The\nsolar arrays are pre-cooled by turning\naway from the Sun a mission-specific number of minutes before the drag pass. The\ndeveloped thermal analysis integrated\nwith the trajectory simulation approach is\nused to fly a temperature corridor instead\nof a heat-rate corridor, which results in\nbetter control of temperatures. This yielded an overall aerobraking approach with\nshorter aerobraking durations and substantial temperature margins.\nThis work was done by Charles Baker,\nMichael Amato, David Steinfeld, and Jeff\nStewart of Goddard Space Flight Center; and Jill\nPrince, Derek Liechty, and John Dec of Langley\nResearch Center. For more information, download the Technical Support Package (free\nwhite paper) at www.techbriefs.com/tsp\nunder the Manufacturing & Prototyping category. GSC-16407-1\n\nLow-Cost, Very Large Diamond-Turned Metal Mirror\nReliable plating and diamond-turning technologies produce visible quality mirrors for\napplications such as semiconductor manufacturing.\nMarshall Space Flight Center, Alabama\nThis innovation is a method for fabricating a low-cost, lightweight, large-aperture mirror by constructing only the mirror substrate by electroforming on a\nmaster form machined from plastic\nfoam. Electroformed tubes of the same\nNiP alloy are installed in the foam mirror substrate master. Installing electroformed NiP tubes in the plastic mirror\nmaster before plating on the plastic\nfoam mirror substrate allows the mirror\nfaceplate and the back surface of the\nmirror to be plated onto the ends of the\nconnecting tubes in the foam plastic.\n\nRemoval of the foam after plating is\ncomplete results in a very stiff and lightweight mirror substrate made only of a\nsingle material. The low cost of the electroformed mirror substrate is made possible by very fast production of a master\nsurface made of plastic foam that can be\nrapidly machined with modern, highspeed machining technology to very\ngood mechanical tolerances in only a\nfew hours.\nNo expensive and laboriously produced master surface is required for\nreplication as in traditional electroform-\n\n38\n\ning of optical mirrors. After the plastic\nform has been encapsulated with a suitably thick NiP deposit, the plastic is dissolved away with a solvent. This leaves a\nhollow, electroformed mirror substrate.\nThe excellent diamond machinability of\nthe high-phosphorus nickel allows the\nelectroformed surface to be diamond\nturned to produce a highly accurate and\nvery smooth optical surface.\nThe extremely low cutting forces of\ndiamond turning can produce visible\nwavelength quality optical surfaces without applying any heavy forces to the mir-\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nProduct(ive) Development\nAt Proto Labs, our quick-turn manufacturing processes allow you to\nfail fast, so you can succeed faster with market-ready parts.\n\nInjection Molding\nPart Design for Dummies\n\nThe road to prototype parts can often be long and\nwinding with other shops. Refer to MFS-33035-1.\n\nVery-High-Load-Capacity Air Bearing Spindle for Large\nDiamond Turning Machines\nMarshall Space Flight Center, Alabama\nLarge-load-capacity oil hydrostatic bearings generate prohibitive amounts of heat in large sizes when run at speeds useful\nfor diamond turning of optical components. The viscosity of\nair is more than three orders of magnitude less than the\nthinnest oil; therefore, the frictional heating of large-diameter\nair bearings is very small and very manageable. A formidable\nmanufacturing problem with large air bearings is that the\nextremely low viscosity of air requires that the thickness of the\nbearing film is also very small. This very small bearing clear-\n\nSmarter Machines.\nCreatively Engineered\nFor Smart Customers.\n\nTHE\nVPD\nPOWER\nDRIVER\nQuality controlled\nautomatic\nassembly tool.\nInstalls screws,\nnuts, rivets, and\npush pins.\nWeldment or robot\nmount for error-free\nfastening.\n\nsales@visumatic.com | visumatic.com | 8 5 9 . 2 5 5 . 7 9 0 7\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-740\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\n40\n\nance of 5\xe2\x80\x938 micrometers means that the required accuracy of\ngeometry and dimensions of air bearing components is\nextremely difficult to achieve.\nIn the case of meter-size air bearing dimensions, the\nrequired precision is comparable to high-quality optical components in most cases because exact dimension is required in\naddition to near-perfect geometry. With current technology,\nthe cost to fabricate, and the time to produce large mirrors, is\none of the limiting factors for many missions. Research tools\nrequire large-aperture, lightweight optics that will perform\nwell in a space cryogenic environment. Currently, no mirror\ntechnology has been demonstrated that meets the mission\nrequirements at an affordable cost.\nDiamond turning has been proven to produce highly aspheric optical contours to visible wavelength tolerances with\nextremely smooth surfaces. Diamond turning has the additional enabling capability to not only produce extremely smooth\nand accurate optical surfaces, but also mechanical attachment\nsurfaces and datums that allow extremely fast and complex optical components to be quickly and easily aligned. The productivity of diamond turning allows the production of quantities of\noptical components with exacting duplication of optical surfaces and datums, which allow \xe2\x80\x9csnap-together\xe2\x80\x9d optical systems.\nThe large diamond turning machines that have been built\nall have oil hydrostatic work spindles that fail in diamond- turning very large optical components because of excessive heat\ngeneration when this type bearing is made large enough to\nhave the required load capacity for very large optics diamond\nturning.\nThe solution to this problem is to make large vertical air bearings with hard metal journals running against porous graphite\nbearing surfaces. Porous graphite air bearings do not friction\nweld in the event of the spindle bearing surface contacting the\nbearing journal surface, and the result is only slight polishing of\nthe graphite surface. The porous graphite flow structure provides extremely uniform flow of the air film, and the tortuous\nflow path acts to damp the flow-induced dynamic instability that\nis a severe problem in both oil and air hydrostatic bearings.\nThis work was done by John Casstevens of Dallas Optical Systems for\nMarshall Space Flight Center. For more information, contact Sammy\nNabors, MSFC Commercialization Assistance Lead, at sammy.a.nabors@\nnasa.gov. It has shown to have an extremely\nstable emittance at lower wavelengths\nwithin the infrared (IR) spectrum,\nwhere energy dissipation is critical at elevated temperatures. The coating has\ndemonstrated increases in surface texturing, and ultimately an increase in\nemissivity when exposed to temperatures up to 2,050 \xc2\xb0F ( 1,120 \xc2\xb0C). It is\nalso stable at continuous run, elevated\ntemperatures, and shows no signs of\nspalling or erosion.\nRadiation-cooled nozzles for liquid\nrocket engines require high emissivity,\napproaching near black body, in order to\nproperly dissipate energy from the component. A common means of increasing\nthe emittance of metallic components is\nto perform a high-temperature heat treatment of the material, allowing the surface\nto oxidize, thus increasing the emissivity.\nHowever, an oxidized surface will reduce\nwhen exposed to the hydrogen-rich steam\nfrom the propellant by-products of liquid hydrogen/liquid oxygen (LH2/LOX)\ncombustion in addition to being reduced\nwhen exposed to a hard vacuum environment. Another disadvantage of the hightemperature material oxidation is reduction of the base material properties.\n\nSome materials, such as superalloys,\nrequire a minimum heat treatment temperature to be obtained; otherwise, the\nstructural properties of the material can\nbe reduced due to carbide precipitation.\nAdditionally, distortion of larger parts\ncan occur at elevated temperatures when\nperforming heat treatment oxidation\ncycles of the material.\nA unique silica-based coating was\nadapted from industry to meet the emissivity needs of the rocket nozzle. The\nCetek coating system is an aqueous-based\nmaterial that is applied using a handheld\nsprayer that could be readily adapted to\nan automatic robot sprayer. Spray application allows even distribution of the\ncoating over the entire surface of the\ncomponent exposed to both hot gas and\nthe vacuum of space. The coating is\napplied in a series of air-sprayed layers\nthat combine to produce a total thickness of less than 0.005 in. ( 127 \xce\xbcm). The\nthin coating allows for minimal weight\nimpact by increasing the emittance of\nthe surface, ultimately decreasing component temperatures and allowing for\nreduced weight of the engine component. The cure cycle for the coating consists of an ambient air cure under controlled humidity conditions, followed by\noven heating at low temperatures for a\nperiod of a few hours.\n\nThe development of this coating was\nnecessary to increase the emittance of\nthe surface of the nozzle extension for\nthe radiation-cooled metallic nozzle\nextension for the J-2X Upper Stage\nEngine. Because the temperatures of the\nnozzle wall are dependent upon the\nradiation effectiveness, emissivity is\nextremely important. The material\nproperties of the nozzle extension are\ndependent on the temperature in which\nthey operate. A lower temperature wall\n(based on emissivity) would allow a higher strength of the base material, and\neventually weight savings of the nozzle\nbased on optimization of the design.\nThus, a higher emittance for the nozzle\nresults in weight savings for the design\nand also a design that yields additional\nthermal margin. This coating successfully completed a series of high-temperature emissivity and durability certification testing and has been shown to survive a rocket nozzle\xe2\x80\x99s extreme operating\nenvironment through both subscale and\nfull-scale testing.\nThis work was done by Paul Gradl of\nMarshall Space Flight Center, Jeff Haynes of\nPWR WPB, and Naiping Zhu of Cetek Ltd.\nFor more information, contact Sammy\nNabors, MSFC Commercialization Assistance\nLead, at sammy.a.nabors@nasa.gov. Refer to\nMFS-32856-1.\n\nCatalyst for Treatment and Control of Post-Combustion Emissions\nThis oxidation/reduction catalyst can be used in diesel and natural gas applications, and in nonautomotive pollution sources.\nLangley Research Center, Hampton, Virginia\nEmissions from fossil-fuel combustion\ncontribute significantly to smog, acid rain,\nand global warming problems, and are\nsubject to stringent environmental regulations. These regulations are expected to\n\nbecome more stringent as state and\nregional authorities become more\ninvolved in addressing these environmental problems. Better systems are needed\nfor catalytic control.\n\n42\n\nIn general, existing catalytic converters used for NOx and He emission control use precious metal (PM) or their\ncombinations as wash coats with various\narchitectures over alumina on ceramic\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nsubstrates to effect catalytic conversion.\nSome of the more common are coatings\nof Pd, Pd/Rh, or Pt/Rh. Existing catalytic converters are less effective for\nremoval of methane HC emissions due\nto the high light-off temperatures for\nmethane on these catalysts.\nThe present invention utilizes at least\ntwo PMs with at least two different metaloxides (for example, tin-oxide plus one\nor more promoters) in a layered matrix\nto convert CO, HCs, and NOx to CO2\nand N2 by oxidation of the first two components (CO, HCs) and reduction of\nthe third (NOx) in a moderately hightemperature gaseous environment (for\nexample, between about 200 to about\n500 \xc2\xb0C) containing excess oxygen.\nPreparation of ruthenium/platinumtin-oxide-based catalyst coatings for\npellets, beads, granules, fabrics, and\nespecially ceramic honeycomb monoliths can be accomplished by successive\nlayering of the desired components, as\nfollows:\n1. A clean, dry substrate is deaerated in a\nsolution containing tin (II) 2-ethylhexanoate (SnEH). The substrate is\nremoved from the solution, and\nexcess solution is removed from the\nsubstrate. Residual solution components are evaporated leaving an SnEH\nlayer on the substrate that is thermally\ndecomposed in air to tin-oxide at 300\n\xc2\xb0C. Several layers are applied in the\nsame manner to achieve the desired\nloading of tin-oxide.\n2. The promoters are added to the catalyst matrix in a similar fashion. For\nexample, an iron oxide promoter is\nadded to an existing tin-oxide-coated\nsubstrate by deaerating in an iron\nnitrate solution, removing excess solution, evaporating the solvent, and\nfinally thermally decomposing the\nnitrate to oxide.\n3. Platinum is added to the coated substrate as above using an aqueous solution of tetraamine platinum (II) dihydroxide or other platinum salt, and\nthen thermally decomposing the salt.\nInstead of the thermal decomposition, a reductive decomposition can\nbe used. For example, the catalystcoated substrate is heated in an atmosphere containing a reducing gas, such\nas carbon monoxide or hydrogen, to\ninduce reduction of the platinum salt\nto platinum. A similar process can be\nused to add the second precious metal\n(i.e., ruthenium), for example, by\nstarting with an appropriate salt, or\nthe mixed PMs may be applied in one\nstep.\n\nThe instant catalyst can absorb the\nNOx species and convert them to NO. As\nsuch, nitrosyl complexation takes place\nwith a noble metal in order to allow it to\nreact with a reducing agent and therefore be converted to nitrogen. Preferred\nmetal-oxide promoters are Fe2O3, NiO,\nand CO2O3. The metal-oxide adduct\nwith NOx is converted to NO on desorption. The NO is subsequently transferred and bound to the PM until\nreduced by CO and HCs to N2. The CO\nand HCs are similarly oxidized by NO or\nO2 and SnO2 at the PM interface site.\n\nThe advantages of the present invention include:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Lower light-off temperatures can\nenable oxidation of methane emissions\nto CO2 for natural gas fueled vehicles\nat lower exhaust gas temperatures.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Lower light-off temperatures for CO\nand HCs enable more efficient catalytic conversion to CO2 at lower\ncost.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A tin-oxide base wash-coat on a ceramic substrate minimizes loss of coating\nthrough cracking, peeling, or dusting\nmechanisms.\n\nCOATED\nAEROSPACE\nDEVICE\nThe Leader in Coating Aerospace,\nMedical, and Industrial Components.\n\n51 Parmenter Road, Hudson, MA 01749\n(800) 258-0110 | www.boydcoatings.com/ADT\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-734\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n43\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMaterials & Coatings\n\nO\n\n90\n\n01\n\nISO 13485\n\nAS\n\nsureffiPM\neffiand\n\nThis work was done by David Schryer and\nBilly Upchurch of Langley Research Center. LAR-16001-1\n\nThermally Activated Crack Healing\nMechanism for Metallic Materials\n\n91\n\n00\n\nIS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The PM coatings are on the top\nface and are enabled to be more\nciently used, thus requiring less\nresulting in lower PM costs.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The mixed PMs result in a more\ncient oxidation/reduction catalyst\nmay be applied in one step.\n\nA thin metallic film of a low-melting-temperature healing agent\nis used.\nLangley Research Center, Hampton, Virginia\nA thermally activated healing mechanism is proposed and experimentally\nvalidated to mitigate crack propagation\ndamage in metallic materials. The protected structure is coated with a thin\nmetallic film of a low-melting-temperature healing agent. To heal or mitigate\ncrack damage, the structure is heated to\nthe melting temperature of the healing\nagent, allowing it to flow into the crack\nopening. Once in the crack mouth, the\nhealing agent has two benefits: (1) by\nadhering to the crack surfaces, the healing agent bridges the crack, reducing\nthe amount of load at the crack tip; and\n(2) any voluminous substance in the\ncrack mouth causes crack closure (premature crack-face contact during cyclic\nloading) that also reduces the crack-tip\nloading.\nThe technology is a coating that could\nslow crack propagation in metal aircraft\nand spacecraft structures. In practice, a\nstructure is coated with a low-temperature healing agent. When a crack is\ndetected, heat melts the healing agent\nand it flows into the crack. As the temperature is reduced, the healing agent\nhardens and heals the crack by reducing\nthe crack-tip stress intensity factor\nthrough closure and bridging.\n\nLaser machining\n\nperfection\nin stainless\nEagle has the skills\nand the technology\nrequired to produce\nall of your custom\nexotic metal parts.\nCome to us!\n\nCurrently, the technology is only\napplicable for cracks that have reached\nthe surface. It requires an external heat\nsource to heat the coated sheet to 250 to\n300 \xc2\xb0F( 120 to 150 \xc2\xb0C) and must be\nprocessed in a vacuum. The coating has\nbeen prototyped on a titanium alloy\nsheet with an indium-tin eutectic alloy\ncoating. Development continues with\nthe ultimate goal of developing the technology into an in situ healing mechanism that can work automatically with\nstructural health monitoring detectors.\nThe goal of the technology is to heal\ncracks in metal on aircraft and spacecraft.\nUltimately, the goal for the technology is\nto incorporate smart coatings that will\nenable truly self-healing materials, which\nwould provide enormous safety and\nmaintenance benefits. Falling short of\nthe ultimate goal of true self-healing\nproperties, the technology would still\nhave value as an in-shop repair tool.\nThis work was done by Stephen W. Smith,\nJohn A. Newman, Robert S. Piascik, and\nEdward H. Glaessgen of Langley Research\nCenter. LAR17681-1\n\nSubsurface Imaging of Nanocomposites\nApplications include sensors and actuators, aerospace\nstructures, and tissue infusion in medical areas.\nEagle Stainless\nTube & Fabrication, Inc.\n\nPhone (800) 528-8650\n\nwww.eagletube.com\n\nLangley Research Center, Hampton, Virginia\nA nondestructive method that is based\non modified atomic force ultrasonic\nmicroscopy (AFUM) methods has been\ndeveloped for characterizing nanomatewww.techbriefs.com\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-735\n\nIntro\n\nrials. The technology allows imaging and\nquantifying of material properties at the\nsurface and subsurface levels. The technology reveals the orientation of nan-\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\notubes within a composite structure and\noffers the ability to determine subsurface characteristics without destroying\nthe nanomaterial structure.\nThe\nmethod is widely applicable for basic\nnanomaterials characterization, including distribution and orientation of particles in a nanocomposite, localized elastic constants and changes in elastic constants, adhesive surface properties,\nsound velocity, and material damping\ncoefficient.\nThe technology is:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Nondestructive: Previous methods\nrequire destructive sampling.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Ubiquitous: A wide range of materials\ncharacterization for nanomaterials is\nenabled.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Elegant: Design is based on modifications to commercially available atomic\nforce ultrasonic microscopy (AFUM)\nhardware.\nThe manufacturing of nanocomposites produced by the embedding of\nnanostructural constituents into matrix\nmaterials has placed increased demands\non the development of new measurement methods and techniques to assess\nthe microstructure physical property\nrelationships of such materials. Although a number of techniques are\navailable for near-surface characterization, this new method allows assessment\n\nActually...\nIt IS rocket\nscience.\n\nof deeper (subsurface) features at the\nnanoscale.\nThis new scanning probe microscope\nmethodology is called resonant difference\nfrequency atomic force ultrasonic\nmicroscopy (RDF-AFUM). It employs an\nultrasonic wave launched from the bottom\nof a sample while the cantilever of an atomic force microscope engages the sample\ntop surface. The cantilever is driven at a\nfrequency differing from the ultrasonic frequency by one of the contact resonance\nfrequencies of the cantilever. The nonlinear mixing of the oscillating cantilever and\nthe ultrasonic wave at the sample surface\ngenerate difference-frequency oscillations\nat the cantilever contact resonance. The\nresonance-enhanced difference-frequency\nsignals are used to create amplitude and\nphase-generated images of nanoscale nearsurface and subsurface features.\nThe technology offers wide-ranging\nmarket applications such as functional\nnanocomposites for aerospace structures, biomedical uses such as infusion\nof tissue with nanoparticles, verification\nof drug delivery to tissue targets, and\nsensors/actuators.\nThis work was done by John Cantrell and\nSean Cantrell of Langley Research Center. Glenn Research Center, Cleveland, Ohio\nA solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC) is an\nelectrochemical device that converts\nchemical energy into electrical power. A\nsolid oxide electrolyzer cell (SOEC)\noperates in a reverse mode of SOFC, and\nproduces O2 and H2 gases. SOFCs are\nbeing developed for a broad range of\napplications including portable electronic devices, automobiles, power generation, and aeronautics. The salient\nfeatures of SOFCs are all-solid construction and high-temperature electrochemical reaction-based operation, resulting\nin clean and efficient power generation\nfrom a variety of fuels. SOFCs of two different designs, tubular and planar, are\ncurrently under development. Planar\nSOFCs offer several advantages such as\nsimple manufacturing and relatively\n\nshort current path, resulting in higher\npower density and efficiency. However,\nplanar SOFCs and SOECs require hermetic seals. Various glass and glassceramics based on borates, phosphates,\nand silicates are being examined for\nSOFC seals. Silicate glasses are expected\nto perform superior to the borate and\nphosphate glasses as sealing materials.\nPlanar SOFCs and SOECs require\nhermetic seals along the edges of each\ncell and between the stack and gas manifolds to separate and contain fuel and\noxidant within the cell and to bond cell\ncomponents together. The requirements for SOFC and SOEC sealing\nmaterials are severe since the cells will\noperate at 600 to 1,000 \xc2\xb0C for thousands\nof hours, with sealing materials exposed\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nFrom One\nEngineer\nTo Another\n\n\xc2\xae\n\nhttp://indium.us/A023\n\nwww.indium.com/a023\naskus@indium.com\nASIA \xe2\x80\xa2 CHINA \xe2\x80\xa2 EUROPE \xe2\x80\xa2 USA\n\xc2\xa92014 Indium Corporation\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-736\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMaterials & Coatings\nto both oxidizing and reducing conditions.\nQuaternary silicate glasses containing\nMgO, CaO, Al2O3, and SiO2 have been\nidentified as seal materials for SOFCs\nand SOECs. These glasses are free of the\ndetrimental constituents such as boron\nand alkali metals. A representative glass\ncomposition in this quarternary system\nhas been prepared by melting the constituents together.
