For thermal\ninstruments, another key requirement will\nbe state-of-the-art, highly sensitive detectors. Thermopiles are prime candidates\nfor high-resolution thermal mapping in\nthe far-infrared (17- to 250-\xce\xbcm wavelength) spectral range. Thermopile detector arrays can be made to be very lightweight and compact. Furthermore, they\nrequire very few ancillary components\n(e.g., readout electronics, optics, amplifiers), which can add to instrument volume and mass. The implementation of\nthermopiles on these missions is likely\nbecause they (1) generate an output voltage that is proportional to the incoming\nradiation within the spectral range being\nmapped; (2) do not require an electrical\nbias or an optical chopper; (3) have negligible 1/f noise; (4) are radiation hard; and\n(5) have a reported specific detectivity of 1\n\xc3\x97 109 cm\xc2\xb7Hz1/2/W at room temperature.\nThe focus of this work is to fabricate\nthermopiles using unique semimetallic\nmaterials developed in the Detector\nDevelopment Laboratory (DDL). Novel\nsemimetallic materials, which have been\ndeveloped for other projects, were evaluated in terms of a thermoelectric figure of\nmerit, as candidate thermopile materials.\nIntermetallic thermopiles, which have\nhigh Seebeck coefficients and, consequently, high detector sensitivity, require\nhighly specialized fabrication techniques\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Dependable, high quality products\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Over 400 technical resources worldwide providing customized and\nstandard thermal solutions to a diverse group of industries\n\xe2\x80\xa2 90 years of industry experience understanding and solving the\ncomplexities of thermal systems\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Dedication to research and the development of innovative technologies\n\n1 / 8 " FIREROD\xc2\xae cartridge\nF RERO D \xc2\xae ca t dge\nO\nheaters provide\np ovide\nprov de\nmaximum perform nce\nm ax mum performance\nm\nwhen\nw hen space is l im ted\ns limited\nmited\n\nS andard\nStanda d he mocouple\nStandard thermocouple\nRTDs are accurate and\ncc rate\nr epeatab e empe a ure\nrepeatable temperature\npeatable\nsensing produc s\nsens ng p oduct\nensing products\ng\n\nM U LT IC L\nMULTIC O IL\xe2\x84\xa2 elec t ic\nMULTICO IL \xe2\x84\xa2 electric\nMULTICOIL\xe2\x84\xa2 e lect r i c\nICO L\xe2\x84\xa2 le c\nlec\nh ea ter e t u e b u\nheate feat ure buil\nhea te r s f eature bui l t - GSC-16837-1\n\nDie-attach Solders\nMade in America\n\nBiAgX\n\n\xe2\x84\xa2\n\nLow-Cost, High-Performance\nMMOD Shielding\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Pb-free and Sb-free\nPb-f\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High melting\n\xe2\x80\xa2D\nDrop-in replacement for\nhigh Pb paste\n\nRelatively inexpensive fiberglass fabric is\nproposed in place of costlier materials.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Low silver reduces cost\n\nLyndon B. Johnson Space Center, Houston, Texas\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Print and dispense versions\n\nHigh-performance micro-meteoroid and orbital debris\n(MMOD) shielding can be constructed from low-cost, off-the-shelf\nmaterials. The advantage in using this innovation is in achieving\nconsiderable reduction in both cost and mass of the shielding necessary to protect spacecraft from hypervelocity MMOD particle\nimpacts. For instance, in a typical application of this technology for\na visiting vehicle used to transport cargo to the International Space\nStation (ISS) over ten years, an estimated $330,000 is saved (at the\ntime of this reporting) in using a less-expensive MMOD fabric over\nconventional materials, and an estimated 2,000-kg mass is reduced\nfrom the MMOD shielding using the materials and techniques\ndescribed here compared to conventional means.\nMicro-meteoroid and orbital debris shields typically contain\nrelatively expensive ceramic fabrics. For instance, the majority\nof the MMOD shields on the ISS US Laboratory module contain six layers of ceramic cloth. The purposes of the ceramic\nfabric in MMOD shields are to break up the incoming MMOD\nparticle or hypervelocity projectile, and cause the debris resulting from the break-up to disperse laterally before impacting\nsubsequent layers of the shield. Ceramics have excellent\ndynamic properties to achieve good projectile breakup, for\neither high-density orbital debris particles or lower-density\nmeteoroid particles, by creating relatively high shock pressures\nin the impacting projectile. Analytical evaluation of various\npotential materials was conducted to determine which material has suitable characteristics for MMOD protection. Based on\nthese evaluations and cost estimates, materials that could provide suitable MMOD protection at lower cost were identified.\nUsing inexpensive fiberglass fabric in place of the more\nexpensive ceramic cloth is currently used to protect the\nSpaceX Dragon commercial cargo vehicle from MMOD particles. This innovation will likely be used on future inflatable\nmodules, and is being considered by Orbital Sciences for use\non the Cygnus commercial cargo vehicle. Other future spacecraft can also use this technology in their MMOD shields.\nThis work was done by Eric Christiansen and Dana Lear of Johnson\nSpace Center, and Frankel Lyons and Bruce Davis of Jacobs\nTechnology. All\ncommercial or custom head-mounted display systems that track the user\xe2\x80\x99s head for\nthe purpose of virtual or augmented reality applications encounter positional display errors due to system latency. A basic\nhead-mounted display (HMD) with headtracking system is comprised of (1) a nearto-eye display, (2) the head-tracking system, (3) one or more symbology or image\nsources, and (4) the display/image\nprocessor. Each element, and the communication among them, contributes a portion to the total latency. HMD system\nlatency manifests as erroneous alignment\nof the virtual and real surroundings as the\nhead is slewed, and is known to induce\nsimulator sickness and other physiological\nissues. Therefore, minimal system latency\nis a design goal to reduce these physiological symptoms. The overall latency budget\nis the sum of time required to measure\nthe dynamic head position, communicate\nthe position to the display processor, compute the scene based on the position, integrate imagery, and render the scene to\nthe display.\nThe Head-Mounted Display Latency\nMeasurement Rig (HeLMR) device (see\n\nfigure) consists of a 3D-printed, anatomically approximate human head that is able\nto \xe2\x80\x9cwear\xe2\x80\x9d all available commercial and custom HMD systems. A high-speed camera is\ninstalled in place of the eye(s) in the correct image plane location. The head is\nmounted on a precision rotary stage that\nmoves the head in a left-right-left \xe2\x80\x9cNo-No\xe2\x80\x9d\nfashion at a precise angular rate. The\ndevice\xe2\x80\x99s native geo-referenced virtual\nimage is rendered and aligned to overlay a\nreal-world reference. Static and dynamic\nlasers are used for reference to aid the\nalignment. The head is moved and\ntracked dynamically while the high-speed\n(300 fps) video image from the camera\nshows the angular skew of the virtual-toactual image due to the system latency. A\nsnapshot image is captured and the angular difference of the virtual image to the\nactual image is measured with computeraided design software. With the error\nangle measurement and the precision rate\ninformation, the total system latency is calculated. The HeLMR device/method\nquantifies the end-to-end latency of the\nentire system and can be mounted on a\nsynthetic torso for in situ measurements of\nflight simulators or other applications.\nInitial testing was conducted on a custom HMD for a lunar lander application.\nAs with a standard tracking HMD system,\n\nsymbology (e.g., terrain, attitude, guidance) is rendered that is referenced to a\nworld coordinate system. As the head is\nslewed, the symbology is misaligned with\nthe surrounding terrain due to the system\nlatency. This head tracker system uses a\nhybrid combination of optical and inertial\nsensors where both the display and tracker are mounted to clear-lens glasses with a\nhead-borne weight of 4 oz. (113 g). The\ntotal system latency of the lunar HWD system was measured to be 67 milliseconds\nwith detailed lunar terrain rendering, and\n33 milliseconds without terrain. This indicates that the latency is content-dependent in addition to several other factors,\nincluding the display update rate. This system is novel with the ability to non-intrusively quantify system latency and has\nbeen successfully used on several HMD\nsystems. The apparatus combines precision COTS equipment in a unique\narrangement, enabling the measurement\nof a difficult-to-quantify parameter.\nThis work was done by Kevin Shelton,\nRandy Bailey, Trey Arthur, Steve Williams,\nLance Prinzel, Lynda Kramer, Denise Jones,\nand Kyle Ellis of Langley Research Center. For\nmore information, download the Technical\nSupport Package (free white paper) at www.\ntechbriefs.com/tsp under the Mechanics/\nMachinery category. LAR-17884-1\n\nThe HeLMR System consists of the head, camera, rotary stage, stage controller, and dc light source.\n\n48\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nWorkspace-Safe Operation of a Force- or Impedance-Controlled\nRobot\nThis technology can be used for automatic control of a robot that may come into contact with an\nobject or operator in its workspace.\nLyndon B. Johnson Space Center, Houston, Texas\nPrecise motion control of a robot by\ncontrolling its various robotic manipulators may be organized by the required\nlevel of task specification. The levels\ninclude object-level control, which\ndescribes the ability to control the behavior of an object held in a single or a cooperative grasp of the robot; end-effector\ncontrol, which is control of the various\nmanipulators such as robotic fingers and\nthumbs; and joint-level control.\nCollectively, the various control levels\nachieve the required mobility, dexterity,\nand work task-related functionality.\nThis invention relates to the automatic\ncontrol of a force- or impedance-controlled robot in an operating environment in which a robotic manipulator of\nthe robot may come into unexpected\ndirect physical contact with an object\nand/or an operator within its workspace.\nA method of controlling a robotic\nmanipulator of a force-or impedance-controlled robot within an unstructured\nworkspace includes imposing a saturation\nlimit on a static force applied by the\nmanipulator to its surrounding environment. Should the robotic manipulator\nunexpectedly contact an object in its\nworkspace, the static force applied by the\nmanipulator is limited. Upon contact, the\nrobotic manipulator proceeds with its\nassigned task without \xe2\x80\x9cfighting\xe2\x80\x9d through\nthe object after the object is encountered.\nThe control strategy also may include\nautomatically executing a predetermined\ndynamic reflex upon a threshold contact\nforce with the object. The dynamic reflex\nalleviates any inertial impulse of the contact that is not already addressed by the\nsaturation-limited static force. The\ndynamic reflex can rely on either dynamic modeling or exteroceptive sensing in\norder to detect the contact.\nThe controller provides a control strategy for the robot using an algorithm. The\ncontrol strategy provides a static compliance phase at all times, and may also provide an additional dynamic reflex phase to\nhelp ensure the safe operation of the robot.\nThe controller may be a server or host\nmachine with one or more microprocessors\nor CPUs. Individual control algorithms resident in the controller may be stored in\nROM (read only memory) or other suitable\n\nmemory and automatically executed to\nprovide the control functionality.\nThis work was done by Philip Strawser of\nJohnson Space Center; John Yamokoski and\nBrian Hargrave of Oceaneering Space Systems;\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nand Muhammad Abdallah of General Motors\nCorp. MSC-25121-1\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-741\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n49\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMechanics/Machinery\n\nCryogenic Mixing Pump with No Moving Parts\nThe pump is self-priming and can efficiently pump two-phase fluid.\nJohn H. Glenn Research Center, Cleveland, Ohio\nRefueling spacecraft in space offers\ntremendous benefits for increased payload capacity and reduced launch cost,\nbut the problem of thermal stratification\nin long-term storage tanks presents a key\nchallenge. To meet this challenge, a reliable, compact, lightweight, and efficient\ncryogenic mixing pump was developed\nwith no moving parts. The pump uses an\ninnovative thermodynamic process to\ngenerate fluid jets to promote fluid mixing. This thermodynamic process eliminates moving parts to generate pumping\naction. Inherent to its design, the pump\nis self-priming and can efficiently pump\ntwo-phase fluid. The device will significantly enhance the reliability of pressure\ncontrol systems for storage tanks.\nIn thermal stratification, vaporized hot\nspots develop inside the storage chamber,\nelevating pressure and creating an overpressure hazard. Mechanical mixing to\nde-stratify the fluid and create a uniform\nbulk temperature in microgravity is an\neffective approach for controlling the\n\n50\n\npressure inside a large propellant tank.\nReliable operation at cryogenic temperature is a challenge for pumps because no\nlubricants can be used. Pumping a saturated cryogenic fluid poses several unique\nchallenges for a mechanical pump.\nExisting pumps for cryogenic fluid transfer require a high degree of subcooling at\nthe pump inlet to prevent pump cavitation or de-priming.\nThe thermodynamic process is well\nknown and has been previously used to\ndevelop heat pumps and refrigeration\ndevices. This innovation applies this scientific principle to two-phase room-temperature and cryogenic refrigerants to produce pumping from cyclic pressurization\nand depressurization of the fluid through\na thermal process. The thermodynamic\nprocess enables efficient use of the heat\nabsorbed during the depressurization\nstep to thermally raise the cryogen pressure during the pressurization step, thus\nminimizing the net heat input to the cryogen due to the pumping process.\n\nOn the pressurization process, the\npumping chamber heats the cryogen,\nincreasing pressure within the pump.\nThe increased pressure displaces some\nof the fluid within the pump.\nOn the cooling cycle, the pumping\nchamber cools the cryogen. The vaporized cryogen in the pumping chamber\nthen condenses, creating a suction pressure within the pump that draws in addition fluid. Check valves are used to\nensure that the fluid is one-directional\nfor efficient pumping.\nThis work was done by Weibo Chen and\nAdam Niblick of Creare Incorporated for Glenn\nResearch Center. For more information, download the Technical Support Package (free\nwhite paper) at www.techbriefs.com/tsp\nunder the Mechanics/Machinery category.\nInquiries concerning rights for the commercial use of this invention should be addressed\nto NASA Glenn Research Center, Innovative\nPartnerships Office, Attn: Steven Fedor, Mail\nStop 4\xe2\x80\x938, 21000 Brookpark Road, Cleveland,\nOhio 44135. Refer to LEW-19140-1.\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-745\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nSeal Design Feature for Redundancy Verification\nThe technology may be of interest to designers of high-altitude aircraft and submarine vessels.\nLyndon B. Johnson Space Center, Houston, Texas\nNASA has requirements for redundant seals to protect human-occupied\ncabin atmospheres, as well as fluid and\ngas systems in space vehicles exposed to\nthe harsh environments. Comparable\nrequirements have been passed down\nto the International Space Station (ISS)\nProgram, and are now levied on the\nOrion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle\n(MPCV).\nThe purpose of this invention is to\nallow independent verification of\nredundant critical seals, which are seals\nthrough which leakage would constitute a catastrophic or critical failure.\nThe problem, in the case of the Orion\nCrew Module (CM) side hatch, is access\nto the seals. In most cases with other\nhatches, access to the seals does not\npresent the same problem because the\narea where the seals are located can easily be bagged with plastic material and\ntape. Bagging the area creates a containment volume for a trace gas around\nthe entire perimeter (inside and outside) that allows ease of seal leakage\nverification using a mass spectrometer\nleak detector (MSLD) per requirements.\nIn the case of the CM side hatch, seals\nare not accessible from either side, and\nare buried deep in the configuration\nwhen the hatch is closed. In the case of\nthe Shuttle Orbiter, side hatch seal leakage verification was performed by pressurizing between the redundant seals,\nand performing a leak decay test. This\nverified that both seals together met the\nleakage requirement; however, it did not\nmeet the requirement to verify each\nredundant seal path independently as\nrequired by the Orion requirement version levied to the program by NASA and\nthus was the driver for this invention.\nThe leak rate specification is much\nsmaller for the Orion vehicle than the\nShuttle Orbiter or any other manned\nvehicle in NASA\xe2\x80\x99s history, and cannot be\nverified accurately by performing a leak\ndecay test.\nIn this invention, a two-beaded silicone rubber molded seal with a metal\nsubstrate retainer is used and the interstitial area between the two seal beads is\nconnected to the leak detector (MSLD)\nvia the test port connector on the CM\nHatch Service Panel. The outer area of\neach seal bulb is shrouded by a helium\n\nenvironment that is contained by the following features designed into the seal\nretainer, which is the core of the invention:\n1. A series of machined through holes\ncalled conductance passageways in the\nmetal seal retainer that are connected\nto one another by conductance grooves\n\nencompassing the entire outer\nperimeter of each seal bulb.\n2. An elastomeric material is permanently adhered to the outer corners\nof the metallic seal retainer. When\nthe hatch is closed, the elastomeric\nmaterial is compressed together creating a containment dam and thus a\n\nGO TEAM USA!\n\nPrecision makes the Difference\n\nPure\nPrecision.\nThe new MVR & AVR\nVision systems from Starrett\nrett\ncombine the strengths of\nhi-resolution digital video\ndeo\ntechnology, powerful yet\nintuitive software, touchchscreen operation and a\nprecision Metrology platform\norm\nto offer a multi-functional\nnal\nmeasurement and inspection\nion\nsystem years to come.\nW tch\nWatch The Video!\nhttp://www.starrett.\ncom/u?NTB0514\n\nInnovation Rules\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nwww.starrett.com\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-732\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n51\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nMechanics/Machinery\ncontainment area for the helium\ntrace gas.\nThis containment volume is evacuated\nand then back-filled with helium to facilitate the test of each seal bead. MSC-24680-1\n\nDexterous Humanoid Robot\nThis robot can replace human workers in dangerous, lifethreatening conditions.\nLyndon B. Johnson Space Center, Houston, Texas\nA humanoid robot has been created\nthat includes a torso, a pair of arms, a\nneck, and a head. The torso extends\nalong a primary axis and presents a pair of\nshoulders. The pair of arms movably\nextends from the shoulders. Each of the\narms is fully jointed. The neck movably\nextends from the torso along the primary\naxis, and has at least one neck joint. The\nhead movably extends from the neck\nalong the primary axis. The head has at\nleast one head joint. The shoulders are\ncanted toward one another at a shrug\nangle that is defined between each of the\nshoulders such that a workspace is\ndefined between the shoulders.\nIn another aspect of the invention, a\nhuman robot is created as described\nabove, but also includes a pair of hands\nand a skin layer. Each of the hands\nincludes at least five fingers such that\neach hand has at least 12 degrees of freedom (DOF). The skin layer substantially\ncovers the robot. The multiple DOF provide the robot with a level of dexterity\nrequired to manipulate standard tools\nand assembly components. The robot\nmay also be configured to achieve\nstrength and sensing levels that are consistent with manipulating substantial\ntools and hardware. The hands may be\nstrong enough to solidly grasp heavy\npayloads, e.g., 20 pounds ( 9 kg) and\nthe like, with the hands in any orientation. The arms, the hands, and the fingers may also be configured to have\nforce control, or the dexterity to manipulate flexible materials, small objects,\nhuman tools, etc.\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-744\n\nIntro\n\nEach robotic joint contains and/or is\ndriven by one or more actuators, e.g.,\njoint motors, linear actuators, rotary\nactuators, and the like. As a result, the\nrobot is configured to perform significant work. Specifically, the robot can perform assembly, construction, and maintenance tasks that are currently performed\nby humans. Additionally, the robot may\nbe configured to be of the human scale,\nallowing the robot to share the same\nworkspaces that are typically designed\nfor human workers.\nThis work was done by Robert O. It is not sufficient to stabilize\nthe kite \xe2\x80\x94 it must be flown in a highspeed crosswind trajectory for power\ngeneration. Additionally, the behavior\nof the kite is highly nonlinear and difficult to model due to flexible structures\nthat assume a shape in response to\naerodynamic loads. In this invention,\nall sensors are kept on the ground,\nallowing the kite to be as light as possible. A fuzzy logic system was chosen to\nperform this control task because a\nreliable model of the kite is unavailable, and the operation of the kite is\nrelatively easy to explain. A commercial, two-line-controlled, ram-air kite\nwas used for developing and testing the\ncontrol system.\nThe system uses measurements of the\ntension in each line, the length of each\nline, and the estimated position of the\nkite from a vision-based tracking system\nas input. It then outputs a commanded\ndifference in length between the lines,\nas well as an average reel rate for both\nlines.\nA predictor-corrector approach is\nused to track the air vehicle. The previously estimated position and velocity are\npropagated to a position estimate.\nDetecting the air vehicle is accomplished using three algorithms: change\nin time of brightness, a change in time\nof hue, and spatial maximum of hue. A\npan-tilt platform is used to keep the air\nvehicle within the camera frame. A fuzzy\nlogic-based controller is used to generate steering and reeling commands for\nthe air vehicle from the position and\nvelocity estimated by the tracking system, as well as the line tension, velocity,\nwind speed, and direction. Groundbased visual tracking is likely to have\nhigher error in localization compared\nto a combination of onboard sensors,\nbut has a significant advantage in\nexpense, complexity, and the weight of\nthe air vehicle. A fuzzy controller has\n\nadvantages in robustness over many\nother control methods.\nThis innovation makes use of\nOpenCV code for some vision processing tasks, Microsoft DirectX and\nXAudio2 code for communicating with\nsome computer hardware, proprietary\nlibraries for communicating with\n\nweather stations, and tension-measuring hardware.\nThis work was done by David North and\nMark Aull for Langley Research Center. Glenn Research Center, Cleveland, Ohio\nThe Lunar Organic Waste Reformer\n(LOWR) is a novel technology to convert organic wastes from human space\nexploration outposts into useful propellant constituents. The LOWR meets\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Trash to Supply Gas (TtSG)\nobjective under the Advanced Exploration Systems Logistics Reduction\nand Repurposing project by integrating\nsteam reformation, methanation, and\nelectrolysis to convert organic waste into\nmethane and oxygen products. At\nreformer temperatures above 700 \xc2\xb0C,\noxygenated steam reacts with organic\nmatter to produce a gas mixture largely\ncomposed of hydrogen, carbon monoxide, and carbon dioxide. After condensing and removing excess water, the\nreformer exhaust gases are fed to a catalytic Sabatier reactor where they are\ncombined with supplemental hydrogen\nat 350 to 500 \xc2\xb0C to produce methane\nand water. The methane product can be\nliquefied for storage.\n\nElectrolysis of water obtained from\nthe reformer and Sabatier reactors provides the supplemental hydrogen needed for methanation, while simultaneously producing oxygen used during\nsteam reforming. Excess oxygen is\nstored as a product. The LOWR system\ndesign requires minimal feed preparation and results in nearly complete\nconversion of feeds to valuable products with minimal consumables, resulting in rapid payback. The thermochemical production of hydrogen in\nthe LOWR reformer leads to significantly reduced electrolysis power\nrequirements compared to other waste\ntreatment methods.\nThe primary application of the LOWR\nis for conversion of organic wastes from\nhuman exploration outposts into valuable propellant components. The\nLOWR reduces risks associated with handling and storing organic wastes while\nrecycling waste constituents into prod-\n\nucts that would otherwise be imported\nfrom Earth.\nAdaptations of the LOWR are suitable\nfor conversion of terrestrial organic\nwastes into fuel for power generation or\ninto feedstock for chemical manufacture. Such usage would reduce effluents\notherwise discharged into the environment while reducing the need for new\nfuels.\nThis work was done by Stacy L. Carrera,\nRobert M. Zubrin, and Mark Berggren of\nPioneer Astronautics for Glenn Research\nCenter. Refer to LEW19093-1.\n\nDigital Laser Frequency Stabilization via Cavity Locking\nEmploying Low-Frequency Direct Modulation\nDirect modulation reduces complexity, volume, and mass.\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, California\nThis project\xe2\x80\x99s goal was to simplify laser\nfrequency stabilization. A simpler system\nwill have many benefits, including reduction of power consumption, complexity,\nvolume, mass, and risk of failure. To\nimplement the Pound-Drever-Hall\n(PDH) technique requires both RF modulation and demodulation electronics,\nincluding an electro-optic modulator, a\nphotoreceiver of sufficient bandwidth to\ndetect the RF modulation fields, demodulation electronics of sufficient bandwidth, and an RF function generator. For\na space mission, this equipment can be\ncostly and power-hungry, in addition to\n\nthe difficulty of being rated to operate in\nthe harsh space environment.\nThe need for laser frequency stabilization will occur in many planned missions\nemploying multiple lasers on multiple\nspacecraft (SIM, LISA, GRACE Follow-on,\nGRACE C). In addition, many laboratory\nmeasurements require laser frequency\nstabilization. This software-based system\nobviates the need for RF components\n(modulator, detector, frequency/function\ngenerator, and associated electronics),\nboth in the laboratory and on a spacecraft, to perform laser frequency stabilization in a digitally controlled system.\n\n54\n\nThe code-based (digital) technique\nwas demonstrated for laser frequency\nstabilization to an optical reference cavity without requiring any RF electronics\nor RF equipment. The process is coded\nusing LabVIEW and employs field programmable gate arrays (FPGAs) for digital input and output from the photoreceiver and laser, respectively. Instead of\nemploying an external phase-modulator\ndriven by a function generator, the digital code directly modulates the laser\xe2\x80\x99s\npiezoelectric transducer (PZT).\nThis work has shown that high laser\nfrequency stability performance can be\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nachieved using optical cavity references,\nwithout the need for the traditional\nPDH RF electronics. This was demonstrated using digital software codes and\ncontrol, significantly simplifying the analog electronics. This technique reduces\nmass and power associated with the RF\nelectronics. This software technique is\nimplemented via hardware that will\nalready exist onboard the spacecraft.\nThis also reduces complexity, power\nconsumption, volume, and risk of failure.\n\nThis work was done by Glenn DeVine,\nBrent Ware, Kirk McKenzie, Robert J.\nThompson, William M. The mercury\nplasma can be generated inside the tube\nwith externally applied RF or microwave\npower. Coils or surface strip electrodes\n\nCustom OEM design\n\ncan be used as a capacitive resonator to\nsustain the plasma. One end of the tube\nis sealed, and the other end of the tube\nis fused with a piece of large-core DUV\nstep-index fiber, where the DUV radiation from the plasma is collected. A gradient-index lens can be manufactured at\nthe output tip of the fiber to deliver the\ncondensed light to the ions. The fiber\noutput can be brought close to the ion\ntrap assembly and coupled through a\nUV window or fiber feedthrough assembly. Alternatively, the miniature plasma\nand delivery assembly could be placed\nentirely in the ion trap vacuum assembly.\nThis work was done by Robert L. Tjoelker,\nEric A. Glenn Research Center, Cleveland, Ohio\nAstronauts suffer from poor dexterity\nof their hands due to the clumsy spacesuit gloves during Extravehicular Activity\n(EVA) operations, and NASA has had a\nwidely recognized but unmet need for\n\nwww.micro-epsilon.com\n\nnovel human-machine interface technologies to facilitate data entry, communications, and robots or intelligent systems control. Refer to LEW-19059-1.\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\nHigh-Pressure, Reduced-Kinetics\nMechanism for N-Hexadecane\nOxidation\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, California\nCombustion of realistic fuels is described by thousands of\nreactions involving thousands of species. Coupling these\ndetailed chemical mechanisms with turbulence simulations is\ncompletely impractical because there is no computer powerful\nenough to solve the resulting equations.\nA reduced-kinetics mechanism has been developed that uses\nonly a few important species to duplicate the detailed mechanism at much lower computational cost. The compact reducedkinetics mechanism has been developed for n-C16H34 oxidation\nthat is based on a local full similarity of the detailed mechanism.\nThe reduced mechanism employs only 20 light species and since\nthe light-species reaction rates are more accurately known than\nthose for heavy species, the present reduced mechanism has a\nsmaller relative error than a reduced mechanism employing\nreactions involving heavy species.\nThe model was exercised in the context of a fixed-mass, constant-pressure reactor, and results were obtained over the\nentire range of equivalence-ratio validity of the detailed mechanism at the high pressures encountered in diesel, gas-turbine,\nand HCCI (homogeneous charge compression ignition)\nengines, and at both cold-ignition and hot-ignition temperatures. The results uniformly show excellent accuracy on the\ntemporal evolution of the temperature, the major species mass\nfraction, and the OH mass fraction when compared to those of\nthe detailed mechanism.\nOne advantage of the developed reduced kinetics is that it\nconsists of the same 20 species for n-C7H16, n-C10H22, nC12H26, n-C16H34, and i-C8H18, making it easy to use a reduced\nmodel for mixtures of species composing a practical fuel.\nThis work was done by Josette Bellan, Panagiotis D. Kourdis, and\nKenneth Harstad of Caltech for NASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory. For\nmore information, contact iaoffice@jpl.nasa.gov.\nThe software used in this innovation is available for commercial licensing.\nPlease contact Dan Broderick at Daniel.F.Broderick@jpl.nasa.gov. Refer to\nNPO-49408.\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-751\n\nMethod of Error Floor\nMitigation in Low-Density\nParity-Check Codes\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, California\nLow-density parity-check (LDPC) codes often suffer from an\n\xe2\x80\x9cerror floor\xe2\x80\x9d in which increasing signal-to-noise ratios do not\nlead to substantially better error rate performance. Error\nfloors of LDPC codes are often not an inherent problem of the\ncode design, but rather, a limitation of the standard decoding\nalgorithms.\nIn this new technology, small but critical variations in standard belief propagation decoding algorithms dramatically\nlower the error floor of LDPC codes, including the set of\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-752\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n59\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nInformation Technology\nAR4JA LDPC codes that are recommended by the Consultative Committee\nfor Space Data Systems (CCSDS), an\ninternational standards organization.\nA modification of standard belief propagation message passing is made in a special way that allows the new decoder to\nconverge to the correct codeword with a\nhigher probability than was previously\npossible. The key innovation that leads to\nthe lower error floors, particularly for the\nrate 4/5 AR4JA LDPC code and for any\nof the AR4JA codes when used with higher order modulations, is the introduction\n\nof a \xe2\x80\x9cpartial hard-limiter\xe2\x80\x9d for decoder\nmessages coming from the check nodes\nof the decoding graph.\nNearly every space mission NASA flies\nuses channel codes to protect the reliability of transmitted bits. LDPC codes are\nthe way of the future, as they are contained in the standards not just for civilian space use, but also for terrestrial WiFi,\nWiMAX, and digital video broadcasting.\nThis work was done by Jon Hamkins of\nCaltech for NASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory.\nFor more information, contact iaoffice@\njpl.nasa.gov.\n\nX-Ray Flaw Size\nParameter for POD\nStudies\n\n2 0 1 4\nThe Authoritative Guide to Global Space Activity\n\nLyndon B. In many\ninstances, the NDE flaw detectability is\ngiven as a flaw size such as crack length.\nThe flaw is either a crack or behaving\nlike a crack in terms of affecting the\nstructural integrity of the material.\nAlthough the crack size relates to structural integrity of the part, it may not be\nthe only factor that affects the flaw\ndetectability for most NDE methods.\nCrack length, depth, and opening may\nrelate differently to the flaw detectability. Part surface roughness and cleanliness may affect the flaw detectability.\nThe proposed flaw size parameter and\nthe computer application described\nhere give an alternative approach to\nconduct the POD studies and apply\nresults of the POD study to reliably\ndetect small flaws through better assessment of effect of interaction between\nvarious geometric parameters.\nMany factors affect X-ray crack\ndetectability. Therefore, crack depth-topart-thickness ratio is not an accurate way\nof defining X-ray flaw detectability.\nModels that relate selected factors such as\nsurface crack dimensions, orientation,\npart geometry, and technique parameters\nto film or image contrast for a simulated\ncrack in a plate were developed. A geometric flaw size parameter is intended to\nbe monotonically related to the POD\nunder these conditions. Single-component simulations lack a detailed representation of the complex composition and reaction mechanisms of realistic fuels, which can contain hundreds to thousands of species. Some species may be\nresponsible for fuel ignition, other species may be the initiators\nof soot formation, and yet others may be involved in surface corrosion, all of which demonstrates the importance of being able\nto track the multitude of species.\nA new formulation has previously been developed that mitigates this problem: the fluid equations are deterministic whereas\nthe composition equations are statistic and the composition is\ndescribed statistically in terms of the molar densities. Thus,\ninstead of tracking individual pseudo-components, this previous\nstudy used a statistical model, based on continuous thermodynamics (CT), to represent the composition by means of a probability density function (pdf) having an assumed shape. This\napproach considerably reduced the computational effort, since\nto obtain information of the type needed to predict mixing and\ncombustion, it turns out that transport equations for only a small\nnumber of the lower moments of the pdf must be solved.\nThat previous formulation was valid when solving all scales of\nthe flow, in a type of computation called direct numerical simulation (DNS). However, DNS requires very fine grids and is not\npractical in engineering applications where coarser grids are\nnecessary for computational efficiency. For these engineering\napplications, the formulation called large eddy simulation (LES)\nis used instead of DNS. In LES, the larger scales are computationally resolved and the small scales, called subgrid-scales\n(SGSs), are modeled. A previous study deriving the LES equaNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-755\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n61\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nInformation Technology\n\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s On\nM A T E R I A L S\n\ntions showed that the SGS terms identified from the conventional methodology to derive the LES composition equations dominated the activity of all other terms, in contrast to the general\nassumptions of LES. Therefore, it was clear that a more adept\nformulation for the composition equations must be found.\nIn this study, a new set of equations was developed that leads\nto a formulation respecting the LES assumptions. To derive\nthese LES composition equations, instead of the Favre mass density weighted filtering typically used to filter the equations, vapor\npartial molar density filtering is used. In this new LES formulation, the composition pdf moment variables are based on a new\ntype of filter akin to, but different from, Favre filtering. For the\nSGS terms in the pdf composition equations that originate from\nthe advective terms, the predictive capabilities of the dynamic\nSmagorinsky and the approximate-deconvolution-model-issued\nSGS models have been evaluated in an a priori analysis, and it was\nfound that the latter showed superior capabilities compared to\nthe former LES model for the composition equations.\nThis work was done by Josette Bellan of Caltech and Michael R. Gloor\nof Institute of Fluid Dynamics for NASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory. For\nmore information, contact iaoffice@jpl.nasa.gov. With\nsupport from the National Science\nFoundation, Ceralink is manufacturing\nUHT ceramics using a new method that\nharnesses the power of microwaves to\ncreate parts faster and cheaper.\n\nNASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, California\nThis innovation creates observations of both targeted geographical regions of interest and general mapping observations, while respecting spacecraft constraints such as data volume, observation timing, visibility, lighting, season, and science priorities. This tool, therefore, addresses both geometric\nand state/timing/resource constraints by using a grid-based\napproach. These set covering constraints are then incorporated into a greedy optimization scheduling algorithm that incorporates operations constraints to generate feasible schedules.\nThe resultant tool generates schedules of hundreds of observations per week out of potentially thousands of observations.\nUsing greedy combinatorial optimization with gridded coverage\nrepresentation, both targeted mapping observations (small geographical regions that can be covered in one or a small number of\nobservations) and general mapping observations (large geographical regions that would take large numbers of observations, e.g.\nhundreds or more) can be scheduled. Using gridded coverage representation of a planetary surface, which maps all polygons\n(regions) into sets of points on a grid, makes polygon intersection\nvery fast, and compiles the coverage problem into a set point covering the problem. At this point, the problem can be attacked\nusing one of a set of combinatorial optimization techniques.\nThis work was done by Steve A. Chien, Gregg R. Rabideau, David A.\nMcLaren, and Russell L. Knight of Caltech for NASA\xe2\x80\x99s Jet Propulsion\nLaboratory. Classic machine vision cameras\nmanage inspection tasks and yield management, while network cameras (also\ncalled IP cameras) handle process monitoring and bringing production to a\nstandstill when necessary.\nIn many ways, the two camera breeds\nare actually similar. Industrial GigE cameras typically work with the same Gigabit\nEthernet technology as IP cameras. Both\ncamera types also use the same technical\nprotocols, including TCP/IP and UDP.\nThe similarities, in fact, make the separate technologies \xe2\x80\x94 both engineered\nfrom the ground up to serve different\npurposes \xe2\x80\x94 easy to combine. Customers\nnow want to implement both camera\ntypes to fit their specific applications,\nand new products are being developed\nthat combine the best features from the\nIP and machine vision devices.\n\nwidths and optimal image quality as part\nof closed image processing systems.\nWhile network cameras compress the\nimage data down to a fraction of its original data volume, in an effort to reduce\nthe bandwidth required to transport\nthat data, industrial cameras deliver raw\nimages. The unprocessed images allow\nusers to review even the most minute of\ndetails, as is necessary for quality inspections or detail measurements. If image\ndata is missing and the error occurs precisely in that area, leading to a false positive by the inspection system, then the\nsystem has failed in its mission.\nIP cameras are also capable of multistreaming, meaning the camera sends\nmultiple streams in different compression formats, such as H.264, MPEG-4,\nand MJPEG. Each end device then ac-\n\ncesses a suitable stream. An operator, for\nexample, can call up a high-resolution\nMJPEG stream offering strong detailing\non his or her monitor, even while a\nspace-saving version in the H.264 format\nis submitted for archiving. Machine vision cameras work with post-processed\ndata that has already been evaluated,\nsuch as production statistics. The devices\nalso optimize equipment performance\nand adapt the equipment to eliminate\nrecurrent errors. In general, the images\nare only stored after inspection.\nReal-time capability, another functional distinction between the two technologies, means that image capture starts\nimmediately after a trigger signal has\nbeen sent to the camera. Image data must\nbe acquired, transmitted, and evaluated\nwithin a set timeframe. The requirements\n\nIP vs. Classic Industrial Cameras\nThe core differences between a classic\nmachine vision camera and a network\n(or IP) device come in the areas of\nimage data compression, multi-streaming, and real-time compatibility.\nIP cameras are specially designed to\nwork with low bandwidths to ensure that\nthey can fit seamlessly in existing networks without overloading them. The\ncamera is configured via a PC on the\nnetwork to send a compressed video\nstream, such as MPEG-4, MJPEG, or\nH.264. The IP camera primarily provides a strong visual impression on the\noperator\xe2\x80\x99s monitor.\nIndustrial cameras, by contrast, are\nengineered to work with large band-\n\nIndustrial camera and IP camera systems are typically found within the same production environment,\ncarrying out their own specific tasks.\n\n64\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nImaging Technology, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nLower production costs through our unique OEM design & feature\nenhancement services. For industrial cameras, realtime images are a prerequisite. When inspecting components in a production\nprocess, for example, the components\nare transported on the conveyor belts at\nhigh speed. For a precise inspection, the\ncamera must acquire the images as\nquickly as the components are being\ntransported. Timing this precisely requires low latency: a small time delay between receiving the trigger signal and acquiring the image. Further, the time\ndelay must not vary; no jitter can affect\nthe moments of image acquisition. For an\napplication with high image rates (e.g.\n300 images per second), the required latency times can only be microseconds.\nSimilar requirements also exist outside\nof the factory, especially in traffic applications. In speed control systems, the camera activity must be synchronized with\nother system components, such as illumination devices. Many classic IP cameras\nare not real-time capable. In typical surveillance situations, such as monitoring\nthe activity on a banking floor, the user\nneeds an automatically captured, continuous stream of images without the need\nto trigger a camera. If a camera is set for\na frame rate of 30 frames (images) per\nsecond, it will internally generate the signals required to initiate an image capture every 1/30th of a second. In some\nother situations, however, it would be desirable to be able to trigger an image capture at a specific point in time. For example, in a traffic control situation the user\nmay want to trigger an image acquisition\nimmediately after a car passes a sensor\non a highway.\n\nConverging the Cameras\nDespite the different technologies\nand objectives, many applications are\ncompatible with both types of cameras.\nThere are now even solutions that operate IP cameras and GigE industrial cameras in one single Ethernet-based system\nusing the same software.\nThe setup is already daily practice in\nthe paper industry. Similar concepts are\nalso in place for steel and foil production.\nEquipment used for these tasks typically\ninvolves numerous sequential process\nsteps spread out over a physical space, including multiple sub-systems that need to\npass products like paper webs to one another. IP cameras monitor the individual\nprocess steps and the transfer of the\nproduct to the next machine. The network devices ensure optimal interplay be-\n\nProduction robots in the automotive industry often combine both camera types: small and light industrial cameras inside the robots, and network cameras for process control.\n\ntween all equipment systems, identify\nthreats and sources of disruption, initiate\na production stop where necessary, and\ngenerally help to optimize the machine\nconfigurations. If, for example, problems\ntend to arise in a given location, then the\nengineers will know to give that area a\ncloser look. At the same time, classic cameras operate within the individual inline\nsystems and test for quality, completeness,\nor dimensions.\nAdditionally, in the world of robotics,\nmachine vision systems have long been\nused to \xe2\x80\x9cteach robots to see.\xe2\x80\x9d After all,\nrobot arms can only perform their highprecision gripping and positioning maneuvers once cameras and image processing tell them precisely where to move.\nSmall, light industrial cameras, like the\nBasler ace, are typically used in robots.\nThe devices are ideal for \xe2\x80\x9cpick and place\xe2\x80\x9d\napplications: gripping, mounting, and positioning tasks during computer chip assembly. Industrial cameras are also\nneeded for measurement and quality controls of the various product characteristics\nbetween and during all production steps.\nSafety during the production process\nis a prime concern for robotics applica-\n\n66\n\ntions. Previously, barriers were built to\nprevent workers from entering into the\ndanger zones containing the rotating robots. The structures were expensive,\ncumbersome, and inflexible. IP cameras\nhave changed the situation fundamentally. The network technologies can now\nbe placed around the robots to create a\n\xe2\x80\x9cvirtual cage\xe2\x80\x9d based on markings on the\nfloor. The camera monitors those\nmarked zones and is authorized to stop\nthe machine if material, or an employee,\nenters into the robot\xe2\x80\x99s working area.\nIn daily use, the boundaries between\nthe two camera technologies are largely\nbecoming blurred. IP cameras, which\nclassically have served in the monitoring\nfield, are more and more frequently\nbeing used in industrial contexts to support process monitoring and production\nworkflows. Camera manufacturers are\nalso offering customers a range of cameras and accessories to ensure an optimal interplay between all components.\nThis article was written by Eva Tischendorf, Senior Communications Specialist at\nBasler AG (Ahrensburg, Germany). However, when the term\nsmart camera is mentioned, a wide variety of ideas still come to mind among individuals because there is no widespread\nagreement upon the definition of what a\nsmart camera technically is. It is generally agreed upon that the basics of a\nsmart camera include not only the image\nsensor, but also some type of processing\nchip: a CPU, DSP, FPGA, or other type of\nprocessing device (see Figure 1).\nToday, however, even an off-the-shelf,\npoint & shoot digital camera has some\ntype of built-in image processing to remove the red eye effect, conduct facial\nrecognition, apply a filter, or perform\nanother type of image processing. So, if\nthe inclusion of a processor along with\nan image sensor is not the defining attribute of a smart camera, what makes a\nsmart camera \xe2\x80\x9csmart?\xe2\x80\x9d The key lies in\nthe output.\nUnlike most cameras, the primary output of a smart camera is not an image,\nbut a decision or information. Since the\nimage processing or machine vision algorithm is done directly on the smart camera, the image does not need to be passed\nonto a PC or another device. Instead, the\nresult of the processing can be passed directly to an operator or another device in\nthe system. For example, a smart camera\nmay be selected for use in an in-line inspection system for a manufacturing line.\nThe output of the smart camera could be\na pass/fail report over a network to a\ndatabase, a digital signal triggering a sorting system, or a serial command to a programmable logic controller (PLC).\nA smart camera is a decision maker.\nStill, if you conduct an internet search\nfor a smart camera, you will receive a\nlarge variety of results with very different features and appearance options.\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s review some essential properties\nof smart cameras and how they have\nprogressed:\n\nS\n\nFigure 1. A smart camera typically includes an image sensor, processor, and some type of I/O. Other\nfeatures can be added, such as direct drive lighting control, industrial I/O, and a display port.\n\nFigure 2. Most smart cameras come with relatively intuitive programming software, reducing the need\nfor an advanced knowledge of programming skills. The program should not only be easy-to-use, but\nshould also be flexible enough to scale to different hardware targets without a complete rewrite.\n\n68\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nImaging Technology, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nOVER 30 DIFFERENT CCD & CMOS SENSORS\nOVER 100 DIFFERENT GIGE CAMERA MODELS\nTHOUSANDS OF SATISFIED CUSTOMERS\nSEVEN YEARS OF GIGE LEADERSHIP\n\nGigE.\nWE PERFECTED IT.\nWE DIDN\xe2\x80\x99T INVENT\n\nAT BASLER WE KNOW WHAT\xe2\x80\x99S PRECIOUS TO OUR CUSTOMERS. A 1 MHz smart camera 15 years ago would have been four times the size and\ncost of a >1 GHz smart camera of today. Many may be surprised when they realize the processing performance on\nsome smart cameras rival what can be done on PC-based systems. An in-line inspection application, for example, that\nrequires barcode reading, multiple geometric pattern\nmatching, color analysis, line detection, and particle analysis can be done with a yield of over 25 parts per second. Additionally, this type of application can include triggering,\nlighting control, communication with other devices, and\ndisplay all within the same compact package. Smart cameras come with a range of available processors, including\nDSPs, PowerPC-class, and Atom-class. There are also options with a mixed offering, such as a CPU with a DSP coprocessor for certain algorithms.\n\nSize\n\nFigure 3. Smart cameras like the NI 1772 camera from National Instruments\nhave high IP ratings for protection against environmental exposures like\ndust and water. Higher IP ratings are beneficial for applications in harsh environments, including outdoor monitoring and industrial vision inspection.\n\nMachine Vision Problem Solver:\nThe Cyton-CXP\n\nOne benefit to using a smart camera is that multiple components of a vision system are integrated into a single package, resulting in a small size and the potential to save a lot of\nspace. The top-of-the-line, high-performance smart cameras\nkeep getting faster without having to affect size, but the devices are also heading further into the low-end market. Reducing the functionality from an all-purpose smart camera to one\nthat is designed for specific algorithms, such as barcode verification/reading and optical character recognition (OCR), reduces the number of components and complexity. With sizes\nsmaller than 55 x 50 mm and weights of less than 60 grams,\nthese types of smart cameras have been significantly growing\nin market availability.\n\nImage Sensor\n\nThe Cyton-CXP is a Machine Vision power house\nThe Cyton-CXP can handle every CoaXPress camera manufactured\ntoday and some that aren\xe2\x80\x99t yet available. With a Gen 2.0 PCIe x8\nback end, it has bandwidth to spare for today\xe2\x80\x99s super high speed\ncameras. BitFlow built on its long history of successful Machine Vision\ntools to design the ultimate high-speed, high-\xef\xac\x82exibility CoaXPress\nframe grabber.\n\n400 West Cummings Park, Suite 5050\nWoburn, MA 01801 USA\ntel 1-781-932-2900\nwww.bit\xef\xac\x82ow.com\n\nSoftware\nMost smart cameras today come with relatively simple-to-use\nsoftware. Advanced knowledge of programming is not required to use the technology, but it is important to keep flexibility and scalability in mind. The investment to learn a new\npiece of software and write an application should be a somewhat long-term one. That is, the software should scale with application requirements and future projects.\nSensor and processor technologies are advancing rapidly, so\nthe best-case scenario takes place when the smart camera model\nImaging Technology, June 2014\n\nFree Info at http://info.hotims.com/49746-759\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\n70\n\nOf course, a smart camera is still a camera and must acquire\nimages. Both CMOS and CCD sensors can be found in smart\ncameras with resolutions of 5 MP, available in both color and\nmonochrome. Smart cameras are not just limited to area scan\nanymore. Line-scan smart cameras are also available with frequencies over 10 kHz. While smart cameras do not cover the\nfull range of options as normal cameras, they feature some of\nthe most popular sensors.\nA key downfall with smart cameras, however, comes when an\napplication requires multiple image sensors connected to the\nsame processing unit. One way to solve the problem is to synchronize multiple smart cameras, but that can add significant\ncost and complexity. In these cases, it is best to explore alternatives, such as compact vision systems or PC-based systems.\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\nand software are well integrated, but not\nexclusive to each other. As a result, if you\nchange smart camera models to a new\nversion or need to move to a different\nhardware platform, such as a PC or operating system, a complete rewrite of the\napplication or IP should not be required.\nThe operating system running on the\ncamera itself can also be a critical factor\nas Windows-based smart cameras connected to a plant network may fall under\nIT restrictions (see Figure 2).\n\nRuggedness\nThe level of ruggedness required is\ndependent upon the environment in\nwhich the smart camera is to be deployed. It is important to note that many\napplications take place in fairly harsh environments. In food inspection applications, for example, the cleaning process\nwashes all parts on the line, including\nthe camera. Smart cameras are available\nwith an IP rating of at least 67, which offers total protection against dust and\nsubmersion in water up to 1 m deep (see\nFigure 3).\n\nIntegration\nThose who have completed a vision\napplication know that vision is often\npart of a much larger system. Since the\nprimary output of a smart camera is a\ndecision, result, or some other information beyond an image, most smart cameras have built-in I/O to communicate\nor control other devices in the system.\nWith industrial automation, the smart\ncamera may need to control actuators to\nsort products; communicate inspection\nresults to a robot controller, PLC, or\n\nFigure 4. Since the primary output of a smart\ncamera is a decision or some type of signal other\nthan an image, many smart cameras include integrated I/O and communication ports: lighting,\ndigital I/O, serial, Ethernet, and USB buses, as\nwell as display ports for user interfaces.\n\nInspection\nSystem?\n\nFREE\nEVERYTHING YOU NEED IS\nAVAI BLE IN OUR NEW\n\nDecision Making\n\nIMAGING OPTICS\nCATALOG\n\nWith the capabilities of today\xe2\x80\x99s smart\ncameras, the adoption of these devices\ncontinues to grow, and newer technologies are being integrated that could help\naccelerate the growth. The devices, for\nexample, are moving into the 3D vision\nspace by providing solutions with multiple image sensors integrated into stereoscope or laser triangulation packages.\nThese days, smart cameras can come in\nall shapes, sizes, and performance levels,\nbut there is still one attribute that still\ndefines them as smart cameras: the ability to perform image processing and\nmake decisions directly on the camera.\nIt is the decision making that makes a\ncamera smart, and with the potential\ncost savings, ease of integration, and increasing performance, smart cameras\nare a cutting-edge option for many vision applications.\nThis article was written by Carlton Heard,\nProduct Engineer \xe2\x80\x93 Vision Hardware and\nSoftware, at National Instruments (Austin,\nTX). With USB and display\nports, smart cameras can completely replace PC vision systems where an operator interface is required; the parts are\nintegrated in a single device.\nOften, for scientific imaging applications, the vision must integrate with motion stages, data acquisition systems, microscopes, specialized optics, and\nadvanced triggering. As a result, many\nsmart cameras today include I/O such as\nindustrial digital inputs and outputs, encoder inputs for image synchronization,\nand communication ports.\nModels are also available with built-in\nlighting, however, an integrated light cannot be independently positioned, and the\nfeature is useless if a backlight is used.\nBuilt-in light controllers, an available alternative, can modify illumination directly\nfrom the smart camera. To effectively\ncommunicate to other devices, more and\nmore industrial communication protocols, including DeviceNet, EthernetIP,\nand serial, are also being supported natively in smart cameras. Today, the district has more\nthan 2,700 K-12 students across six\nschools, including a high school, a middle-intermediate school, an elementary\nschool, a primary school, a pre-K center,\nand a School of Success.\nWith safety being a core part of LEISD\xe2\x80\x99s\nvision statement, it is no surprise that district leaders strive to do all they can to ensure the safest school environments possible. In early 2013, however, the district\nrealized that it needed to make some serious improvements to its security infrastructure. According to Matt Fry, LEISD\nMedia Coordinator, 40 analog cameras\nwere being used district-wide, and some\ncampuses were not covered at all.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe cameras offered some basic coverage outside, but not a lot inside,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cWorse yet, they had very poor picture quality and big lag time lapses between frames, so we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really use\nthe video for much. We knew it was time\nto update to a more modern, digital solution with a lot higher resolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\nFry took these challenges to the TCEA\n(Texas Computer Education Association)\nconference in February 2013, where he\nmet security networking experts from DLink, a developer and manufacturer of\nnetwork solutions and IP surveillance,\nwho proposed a follow-up meeting to discuss the district\xe2\x80\x99s specific needs.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe also wanted a system that was scalable so we could take it district-wide,\nplus provide the ability to do remote\nmonitoring,\xe2\x80\x9d Fry said.\n\nThe Solution\nShortly after the conference, the team\nbrought in a local technology integrator,\nCMC Network Solutions, and met on site\nto examine the district\xe2\x80\x99s existing network infrastructure and physical layout,\nwhile discussing its specific IP surveillance requirements.\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmong other things, the district\nneeded the ability to archive video data\nfor up to 30 days, so they could review\nincidents after the fact, if necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLiberty-Eylau Independent School District utilizes D-Link switches (above) in conjunction with an array\nof indoor/outdoor cameras and SAN arrays for its surveillance technology.\n\n72\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nImaging Technology, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nsaid Scott Broekemeier, Principal at the Plano, TX-based\nCMC Network Solutions. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter the site survey, we created a\ncustom design that provided the performance, resolution,\nand coverage they desired.\xe2\x80\x9d\nHaving reviewed bids from multiple providers, LEISD ultimately chose the technology from D-Link: IP cameras, switches,\nvideo storage, and access control. Box\n3525, Northbrook, Il 60065.\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n79\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nSpinoff\n\nSpinoff is NASA\xe2\x80\x99s annual publication featuring successfully commercialized\nNASA technology. This commercialization has contributed to the development\nof products and services in the fields of health and medicine, consumer goods,\ntransportation, public safety, computer technology, and environmental resources.\n\nHome Air Purifiers Eradicate Harmful Pathogens\nAn air scrubber used in space station plant growth helps you breathe easier at home.\n\nI\n\nn the 1990s, NASA scientists were\nthinking of what astronauts would\nneed to survive long-term missions to\nthe Moon and other planets. One\nimportant requirement was a dependable source of food, which could be\naccomplished by astronauts growing\ntheir own produce in space-age greenhouses. But cultivating crops in a sealedoff environment results in the buildup\nof an undesirable gas called ethylene.\nPlants release the odorless, colorless\nfume into the air, which has the unfortunate effect of accelerating decay, hastening the wilting of flowers and the ripening of fruits and vegetables.\n\nMizuna lettuce growing aboard the International\nSpace Station. Ethylene scrubbers are used to\nremove the gas from the air, keeping vegetables\nfresh in space.\n\nTo address the problem, Marshall\nSpace Flight Center\xe2\x80\x99s Space Product\nDevelopment Program funded the\nWisconsin Center for Space Automation\nand Robotics at the University of\nWisconsin to develop plant growth chambers that included an ethylene reduction\n\ndevice. In this \xe2\x80\x9cscrubber,\xe2\x80\x9d air is drawn\ninto tubes coated with thin layers of titanium dioxide (TiO2). When an ultraviolet (UV) light source located in the tubes\nstrikes the TiO2, the ethylene gets converted to water and carbon dioxide, both\nof which are beneficial for plants.\nThe ethylene scrubber was first used\nin 1995 aboard a Space Shuttle\nColumbia mission, successfully preserving a crop of potato seedlings. Updated\nversions of the device were subsequently\nflown on several missions to the\nInternational Space Station.\nWhile NASA\xe2\x80\x99s main objective was to\nget rid of ethylene, the scrubbers were\ncapable of purging all kinds of unwanted organic particles from the air.\nRecognizing its powerful air purification\nabilities, KES Science & Technology\n(Kennesaw, GA) licensed the technology\nfrom the University of Wisconsin. The\ncompany then partnered with Akida\nHoldings (Jacksonville, FL), which marketed the technology as Airocide.\nIn 2013, Airocide finally found its way\ninto people\xe2\x80\x99s homes when Akida\nHoldings adapted the technology for\nhome use by developing an eye-catching\nportable unit with enough power to\npurge an entire room of pathogens. The\nAirocide unit is the only air purifier that\ncompletely destroys airborne bacteria,\nmold, fungi, mycotoxins, viruses, volatile\norganic compounds, and odors. Grocery\nstores and produce distribution facilities\nnow use it, in addition to a host of wineries, distilleries, and floral businesses. The\ndevice has also found its way into refrigerators used for both homes and for dis-\n\n80\n\nThe Airocide home unit can be placed on a flat\nsurface, hung on a wall, or mounted to a floor\nstand.\n\ntributing food aid to remote towns. In\nhospitals and clinics, Airocide\xe2\x80\x99s germkilling properties are used to purge the\nair of harmful bacteria.\nThe home Airocide unit has a sleek,\nglossy, rectangular body and can either\nbe mounted on a floor stand or hung on\na wall. Airocide works differently from\nHigh-Efficiency Particulate Air (HEPA)\nfilters, which are designed to capture\nparticles that are 0.3 microns across and\nlarger. Airocide is simple to operate \xe2\x80\x94\njust plug it into an outlet, turn it on, and\nforget about it. The only upkeep\nrequired is replacing the reaction chamber, which houses the UV light source,\nevery 12-14 months. There are high- and\nlow-intensity modes, as well as automatic, which alternates from high in the day\nto low at night.\nAccording to users, the product helps\nrelieve symptoms associated with asthma, allergies, and sinus problems.\nVisit http://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2013/\ncg_4.html for the full story.\n\nwww.techbriefs.com\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\nNASA Tech Briefs, June 2014\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\n" Access to the right technology is key\nto creating an innovative design.\n\nIt\'s great to be an engineer."\n\nENGINEERS\n\nSTART HERE.\nResearch. Right Expertise.\nDelivered Right Now.\n\nAgilent\nBasic Instruments\nMay\xe2\x80\x93July 2014\n\nUpgrade to your\nUltimate Scope\n\nCheck it out.\n\nGet Up to 16 scope\napplications for the\nprice of 1!\n\nNEW higher frequency\nwaveform generator\nfrom Agilent.\n\nSee promos on page 8 or\nscan QR Code for complete\npromo listing.\n\nOur Featured\nDistribution Partners\nmake it easy to buy.\nPAGE 3\n\nIntro\n\nCov\n\nToC\n\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\x93\n\nA\n\n\xe2\x9e\xad\n\n\xe2\x9e\xae\n\nSPOTLIGHT\n\nEliminate the uncertainty and generate the signals you expect to see\nWhen your tests demand a precise and predictably shaped signal, can you be\nsure your signal generator is outputting the signal you expect?\nThe drawbacks of DDS\nDirect digital synthesis (DDS) is a simple and inexpensive method that produces\napproximations that are acceptably close to the ideal for many applications, which\nis why lower-cost waveform generators typically use this technology.\nHowever, these signals are indeed approximations, which can create problems\nwith harmonic distortion, jitter, aliasing, and even skipped points in the waveform.\nBecause DDS generators have a \xef\xac\x81xed sample clock, if they output one unique\npoint for each sample clock, they would be able to output only one frequency.\nWhen a lower frequency is needed, the generator needs to use many clock cycles\nto output a single point and will repeat points. When a higher frequency is needed\nand the generator can\xe2\x80\x99t output all of the points in waveform memory, it will skip\nover some points. The higher the frequency, the more points are skipped\xe2\x80\x94and\nthe less the output resembles the signal you expect to see.\nMoreover, DDS generators use internal algorithms to determine which points are\nskipped. These algorithms put a priority the phase of a signal, which results in\nsignals that don\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily skip the same samples in every cycle.\n\nTo learn how to overcome your function generator challenges with\nTrueform technology, view videos and download free measurement briefs,\nvisit www.agilent.com/\xef\xac\x81nd/trueformTC\nFigure 1:\nSignals at the same\nfrequency created by a\nDDS generator (upper\ntrace) and a Trueform\ngenerator (lower trace)\n\nFigure 2:\nThis 40 MHz sine wave\nfrom the 33600A shows\nless than 800 femtoseconds of jitter.\n\nTrue signals with Trueform technology\nTrueform waveform generators use proprietary technology that allows waveforms\nto be expressed with the same shape, regardless of frequency. Designers working\nwith complex waveforms can use Trueform generators to generate signals with\ncomplex modulation and abnormalities. Digital waveforms with transients and\npulses can be reproduced with the same characteristics every time.\nFigure 1 shows an example of the dramatic differences between DDS and\nTrueform waveforms. Note the three small peaks in the Trueform signal and compare the DDS output; these signal characteristics are distorted or missing entirely.\nDramatic reduction in jitter\nAnother key bene\xef\xac\x81t of the Trueform architecture is extremely low jitter.\nCycle-to-cycle jitter for the new Agilent 33600A Series, for example, is speci\xef\xac\x81ed\nat less than 1 ps for all waveforms, including arbitrary, sine, and square waves.\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s about 200 times less jitter than DDS-based generators (Figure 2).\n\nFigure 3:\nA DDS generator\n(upper trace) was\nunable to output all\nseven aberrations in\nthis 2 MHz arbitrary\nwaveform.\n\nMissing the point\nFigure 3 illustrates the problem of missing points in DDS outputs. At 1 kHz, both\ngenerators were able to create an accurate waveform with all seven aberrations, but when the frequency was dialed up to 2 MHz, three of the seven signal\nfeatures disappeared from the DDS output. At even higher frequencies, the aberrations can be become intermittent or distorted\xe2\x80\x94or all of them can disappear\ncompletely.\n\nAgilent\n\nAgilent and our network of Agilent Authorized Distributors\nhave teamed up to provide fast, easy access to the\nworld\xe2\x80\x99s largest selection of off-the-shelf T&M\ninstruments.
